		<username>TOMASBBloodhound</username>
		<score>0.6</score>
		<shortDescription>Good acting, but too long and too much manufactured family angst.</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>They had it right in terms of casting, anyway. Everyone here seems perfectly cast, even Thornton, who is under-utilized. The performances are rich, and powerful enough to carry this soap opera quite a ways, but ultimately not far enough. Robert Downey Jr. plays a stereotypical hotshot defense attorney who is forced to try to reconcile with his estranged father when dear old dad is accused of manslaughter. Downey's father, played by an increasingly geriatric Robert Duvall, is a no-nonsense small town judge who may or may not have intentionally run down and killed a man with whom he has a troubled history. Did dad do it? And if so, was it intentional? Hard to know, when the old timer's memory is slipping on top of everything else.The trial takes up a good deal of screen time, and this is a good and bad thing. The good thing is it gives us a furlough from the back and forth family angst between Downey, his father, his brothers, his wife, his ex-girlfriend... you name it. Downey has a lot of issues to work out with a lot of people. The tone of this film is relatively serious, and it definitely attempts to tug at your heart strings. Somehow, a lot of this aspect of the film just seems too forced. How many people in real life are so obstinate that they don't speak to their parents for twenty years? Or their brothers? It just isn't believable. Their constant arguments make a long movie seem longer. The bad thing about the trial is that it winds up being pretty anticlimactic. The verdict is predictable, any anyone hoping for a revelation that will exonerate the old timer will be disappointed. Maybe having things all cleared up by an unexpected technicality such as seen in My Cousin Vinny would have made things worse, though. Overall, it just seems like the trial should have had more of a payoff. The film also loses points for a subplot involving Downey possibly making out with a girl who is actually a daughter he never knew about. Logically, it seems like she could be none other than his, but the movie seems to chicken out in that respect. The actual revelation of her father's true identity is pretty hard to believe. There are some good points here, too. Besides the strong acting and casting, the film also makes no bones about the horrors of the advanced stages of cancer. Duvall may never get the chance to put so much of himself into a role ever again. He earned his nomination. Vera Farmiga is also a welcome sight in just about any role. The verdict.... they should have gone for more of a comedic angle. Downey, Duvall,... all of these people could have made it more enjoyable if they just relaxed the film's tone a bit. I don't think Terms of Endearment had so much angst! Watch if you must. 6 of 10 stars. The Hound.</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>Well made drama is missing a certain spark</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>When the Academy Awards go ahead on the weekend and beam across the world to countless movie fans, they'll be barely noticing The Judge's 1 solitary Oscar nomination, a nomination for the always watchable Robert Duvall in a role that is baited perfectly for the token older statesmen nomination that occurs seemingly every year. This one lonesome nomination for a film that many talked up as a major contender mid-year is the perfect summation for a film that promised so much, yet delivered so little.Filmed beautifully yet unquestionably without flair, acted well but by actors we've all seen be better, directed in a workmanlike yet without a beating pulse fashion by previous comedy developer David Dobkin and written often smartly yet frequently with a sense of coldness by Nick Schenk and Bill Dubuque, The Judge does so many things right yet so many things equally so-so. The Judge is a film tailor made to feature cliché after cliché and not once do you not know where things are headed and for a film bordering on 140 minutes the various plot strands and side turns culminate in a film that has a lot to say without ever developing them to their fullest extent which is a real shame considering the pedigree and story potential on offer.With a cast of experienced, professional performers at his disposal, Dobkin really struggles to get much out of his troops, even Duvall despite the Oscar nomination is slightly off the boil in a role that at times can become downright embarrassing for the poor old guy. Downey Jr. in another take on his Tony Stark persona really needs to take a step back from his fast talking, wiser than all rich guy roles that have seen him become almost a self-parody of the performances that saw him back to the A-List. Downey Jr. and Duvall share some nice moments together whether they are bickering or enjoying each other's company, but as solo turns there is nothing much to ride home about. It's hard to pinpoint how such a varied cast (which includes Vera Farmiga, Vincent D'Onofrio and Billy Bob Thornton), a decent story set up and a pedigree production could produce such good but disappointingly average final product.The Judge perhaps needed a wiser head to adjudicate it's makings, Dobkin seemingly struggling with his newly found dramatic mojo, as it's not hard to see why The Judge became such a non-event upon release that has seen it disappear from memory, awards shows and critics minds mere months after it was a golden child of the hype train. As someone said to me, "I can see The Judge being played on mid-day TV in a few years' time" which is a verdict that perfectly sums up the crimes of a film that could have been so much more had it mastered anyone of its countless ambitions. Harsh judgements these maybe, but when you've got something that looks good on paper, the expectations come with it.3 hosing offs out of 5 For more movie reviews and opinions check into -www.jordanandeddie.wordpress.com</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>Despite A Reasonably High Rating, The Judge is Still Underrated.</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>The Judge stars Robert Downey Jr and Robert Duvall. Need I say more? The extraordinary gifted and talented Roberts lead an all-star cast that includes Billy Bob Thornton, Vera Farmiga, and Vincent D'Onofrio, and Dax Shepard. I will agree with most haters that the courtroom portion is a bit weak and and Downey Jr is too Tony Starky, but who cares? I would honestly say the only flaw is that it is too long. This could easily have been less than two hours.Downey Jr plays hotshot lawyer from Chicago named Hank Palmer who returns to his suburban Indiana hometown for his mother's funeral and must contend with the town and family he left years ago. I like this early portion because it shows very beautiful shots of the sun beating down on nature and rivers that make it very nice to see. We also get a good look at who these characters are. Downey Jr's comeback role of Tony Stark has made him star as all-too similar characters since Iron Man's release, so his role as Hank Palmer does lots of wisecracks. His father, Judge Joseph (Duvall) as been accused of murdering a young man he hates and Hank jumps to represent him. Hank bears a slim chance of his father being let off due to apparent evidence and telltale signs. Hank and Joseph rebuild their lives together and rekindle their relationship as father and son. Joseph's physical and mental health is deteriorating and it is sad to see what is happening with him. I wouldn't call it melodrama, just great acting. When it gets to the courtroom portion, yeah it's not complex or as good as To Kill A Mockingbird or 12 Angry Men, but who cares? The plot and characters have been firmly established and deeply examined to make the court part great. Take the courtroom part by itself and it's weak, but you can't have one without the other. The courtroom portion is not to be informative about the law or to centre the movie on, it is there to help our two stars grow and be closer than they've ever been. This is the perfect father-son movie to watch. Downey Jr and Duvall are captivating in this wonderful film.</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>A lesson of cinema</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Watching this film, and reading a series of reviews, has taught me something i thought i already understood but now is already crystal clear.When you are watching a film, and you want to analyze it, there are tons of thing you can think of: the cinematography was great, the music was excellent, the script is amazing, the cast and their performances are marvelous, the director's view is clear, etc... And that is one of the great things of Art, it is so great that you can find a million persons and every single one of them would tell you something different about the movie, and at the end of the day, that's what matters the most, if you like it or not.When "The Judge" credits started going down, i thought "Man, this may be one of the best movies of year", then i went to some reviews sites and start realizing that a lot o people were saying that the movie was to long, that it was a living cliché, that it was a poorly made court drama film, and as i said before, there is no problem in either if you hate it or love it, but just don't hate it for the wrong reason. The cinematography was great, you can immediately get the truly celluloid look and the tone they were looking for with great placed lighting, the score was accord to the movie, it was not overwhelming and it drive us perfectly in what is the main theme of the movie, WHICH IS NOT A JUDGE ON TRIAL FOR MURDER, if not the relationship between a father and his son, behind that there is trial, that works as our transport in this emotional growth that each character has, emotional growth that is perfectly performed by this two great actors which should be both at least nominated for the Academy, the script and passing are good, there is no way in that you could put every movie in a 90 pages script, some times you just have to tell more to show the story in the way you want to show it. This movie is touching, realistic and well told. I know this is not the most original idea in the movies universe, but that doesn't turn it in to a Cliché. Guillermo Del Toro once said that every movie story is already told, what matter is the way in that you tell it, and it can't be more true. No matter what film you see in this days, i'm 100% sure that if you look in the past, you'll find at least one that has the very same base story, what changes is the way in that it is being told to you. If you think like that, ?every movie would be a cliché don't you think?, I don't think so. I'm a 18 years old Film student and i've watch a lot of films, and at least for me, there has being a long time since a movie show us this kind of son-father relationship and emotional growth in such a touching way, this is by far one of the greatest movies of the year and you should not hesitate while thinking if it worth your time or not. 		<date>2015-01-05</date>
		<username>Franco Strydom</username>
		<score>0.8</score>
		<shortDescription>Well written, potent casting, impeccable cinematography; an effective film.</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>"The Judge" (Dobkin 2014) is a human story about everyday people living their everyday lives and it is beautifully told. I say everyday lives but obviously any film aimed for popular consumption will have a certain amount of 'not your average day'. That being said, this is a simple story told in a simple way. Contrary to my usual opinion this works really well as the simple plot leaves space for story that's generated between the characters. Also incredibly refreshing is the fact that the characters develop from beginning till end and that development isn't limited to the leads. All the secondary characters keep developing till the end of the film which keeps you surprised and the story interesting.The endings of films like these I often find a little awkward, the usual suspect for an ending is 'now that all the challenges have been dealt with everyone returns to what they were doing before', which can be boring or too ideal; let's be honest happy endings are disappointing in dramatic films. This film doesn't suffer from this. The ending leaves the viewer with a thought for what the character(s) might do next and not just 'they return to their lives'.The mention of the ending brings up the cinematography; the closing shot is incredibly well executed. Without a word the camera gives you a power relation, a personal revelation, a sense of closure and/or a wonder for what might happen next, all in some beautiful framing, blocking and editing. Beyond that scene the film is littered with beautiful camera work that contribute to the film beyond just showing the audience what's happening. The framing and blocking of action is artistic in quite a few scenes but not artistic in that it's trying to be; the honest artistic where the Director of Photography is on- board with the story and doing his creative part in telling the story though his medium. Now, to nitpick the photography I will mention that some of the aerial shots feel a little out-of-place. Like the rest of the film the aerials are beautifully shot and the movement is fluid, and there is almost the problem. We're not used to seeing traveling shots with perfect movement, stability and fluidness so when there's a scene where we're keeping pace with a bicycle and the cut of the edit is between two different aerial shots, it draws attention. I would have loved one of these cameras to be on a different rig ? car mount or static wide.The casting was brilliant, I enjoyed all the characters and I enjoyed all the actors as their respective characters; the casting definitely plays to the strengths of the actors and the ensemble of actors plays to the strength of the narrative. As with any narrative story not all characters are made equally but "The Judge" (Dobkin 2014) allows each character a story, with emotion, cognition and background to the story and as an active viewer, that's lovely as it allows you to see beyond the frame on the screen with ease.I really enjoyed watching this film, I walked into the cinema with no specific expectation and I walked out having experienced an emotional journey with some engaging characters wondering what would be next for them.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<fullReview>Exploring his past isn't easy for Hank Palmer, a high-powered Windy City lawyer who has carved his niche defending wealthy criminal types. This role is quite a contrast to the bombast of Tony Stark and even with some of the swagger of Stark hovering this is a much more nuanced presentation. Downey's troubled journey and renaissance in real life, likely, have forged an excellent performance here. Now, this isn't to say this yarn isn't puffed up with all the clichés' such as old girlfriends, confrontations with testosterone and beer enhanced red necks?but, The Judge has it's heart in a place where this is just a little garish color around a much better picture that is slowly painted. Robert Duvall as Judge Palmer is the field from which the fruits of Hank Palmer spring forth. Duvall, the consulate actor he is, delivers the character of Judge Palmer to perfection. Even in anger Judge Palmer never comes off as anything but poignant.This movie delivers with heart and soul. The cast couldn't be better, the cinematography is as beautifully framed as a Normal Rockwell Saturday Evening Post cover. The story itself, well it's never more sensational than real which is to say it works on a very humanistic level. The Judge humbly weaves it's magic by not overreaching and succeeds by just showing a family's triumphs and tragedies that ring true.</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>Nothing new to see here folks (good film and liked it, but not THAT superb)</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Robert Downey Jr. and Robert Duvall elevate an average and overdone premise into a heartfelt and engaging film in The Judge.Hank Palmer is a big city lawyer on the west coast, and has a tumultuous and strained relationship with his father, a prominent judge in a tiny town. Alas, he returns to his small hometown in the Midwest when his family needs him and tries to restore his relationship with The Judge.All in all, The Judge is pretty unremarkable and quite cliché. Troubled son of a controlling father follows in said father's footsteps in an attempt to prove himself and his worth. Son returns home due to tragedy after long separation and while home old wounds open up, clarity in perspective is attained and contact repaired.The story conceived by Nick Schenk and David Dobkin is far from original and frankly has been done to death. Dobkin further directs the feature film written by Schenk and Bill Dubuque. The cinematography in The Judge is clear, direct but literal and uninspired.It is thanks to Paul Schnee and Kerry Barden that The Judge excels in any notable fashion. The casting of the film is remarkably stellar, enlisting a whose-who of talented actors within their craft. Robert Downey Jr. plays the arrogant city lawyer with a delinquent past. Robert Duvall is the domineering father but well respected pillar of the small community. Even the supporting roles are sublime, with Vera Farmiga, Vincent D'Onofrio, Billy Bob Thornton, Dax Shephard, Jeremy Strong and Leighton Meester round out the cast.The leads are so perfectly cast that the casting single-handedly elevates this average drama to a compelling narrative. The beginning of the film is a bit rough but by the end I was engaged and connected with the characters' transformations.For more reviews of recent releases, please check out our website.</fullReview>

		<date>2015-02-04</date>
		<username>jadepietro</username>
		<score>0.5</score>
		<shortDescription>The Verdict is In</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Rating: &#x2606;&#x2606; This film is not recommended.There is a scene in The Judge where the two characters, a father and son, walk in different directions from each other. That serves as a heavy-handed metaphor for their stormy relationship (and that's not even counting a real storm later in this film either). That moment sums up this overstuffed film whose many subplots go in all sorts of different directions.The filmmaker, David Dobkin, is addled with a weak screenplay that can't seem to make up its mind whether it wants to be a courtroom drama or a family melodrama about a dysfunctional Midwest clan.The main plot of the film seems to be a) a slick city lawyer named Hank (Robert Downey) returns home to an ailing and distant father, Judge Joseph Palmer (Robert Duvall) who b) may have killed a man during a hit and run accident. Those two stories never gel. Add to that, c) a romantic entanglement with a former girl left behind with a past secret, d) an unhappy brother who never amounted to much, d) a low-functioning younger brother who is obsessed with his camera and e) another ambitious lawyer out to win his case against Hank's father. Neither do all this other plot points come together well.What does work is the fine acting by the Roberts. Downey takes on a serious acting role, a nice change of pace from his action hero work, and reminds moviegoers how skillful an actor he can be. Of course, playing opposite an acting legend like Mr. DuVall does up the game. Duvall reprises his Great Santini character as the dominating father figure, but he excels in his quieter scenes as a man coming to terms with his own morality. Although the film cannot stay clear of clichés, their scenes together still resonate with tension.The other actors are left with underdeveloped characters to play. Vincent D'Onofrio, Vera Farmiga, Jeremy Strong, Dax Shepard, and Billy Bob Thornton bring their A game to their film roles.The problem with The Judge is its troublesome screenplay by Nick Schenk and Bill Dubuque that constantly rambles from one subplot to the next and never builds to a satisfying conclusion. The family drama is predictable fare and the courtroom case is fairly predictable also. David Dobkin's direction keeps everything moving but with nowhere to actually go. So what we are left with is a well acted film in search of a better script.I rule that less would have been more in the case of The Judge. GRADE: C+Visit my blog at: www.dearmoviegoer.comANY COMMENTS: Please contact me at: jadepietro@rcn.com</fullReview>
	</review>

		<username>Matt Wyatt (Matt@yourturntopick.com)</username>
		<score>0.6</score>
		<shortDescription>Objection Your Honor, Unnecessary Uncomfortable Plot Twist.</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>In the Judge, Robert Downey Jr. plays slick high priced attorney Hank Palmer. Hank has recently been informed that his mother has passed, so he travels back home to a small town in Indiana to visit his estranged family. His father (Robert Duval) Joseph is the Judge in the town and has been for decades. The night after the funeral, Joseph drives out to pick up some groceries in the rain. The next morning the police arrive to inform everyone that a man had been struck and killed on the side of the road by a car matching the description of Joseph's Cadillac. Joseph then is made to stand trial with Hank his estranged son as his representation. This movie had all the great makings of an awesome court room drama. The courtroom used in this is one of those old wood bench, to Kill a Mockingbird sets. A romanticized courtroom look that is often fictionalized but rarely exist outside of museums. This movie has a stellar supporting cast including Vincent D'Onofrio, Vera Farmiga and the legendary Billy Bob Thornton. There is a great plot set up with a decent air of mystery to uncover. And to top it off two great actors in a dramatic head to head, Robert Downey Jr. versus Robert Duval. Robert Downey Jr has recently been named the highest paid actor in Hollywood, Mr. Iron Man. I love to watch RDJ do his thing on screen. Always verbose, sharp witted, playing the smartest guy in the room. But do we ever get any range out of him? Are we really getting our money's worth? I don't know if he can turn it off. He is convincing in his roles, but never are those characters diverse. There is always a little Tony Stark in everything he plays. As for Robert Duval, the man is a legend. The academy has elected him one of this year's best supporting actor nominees. ( I think he will lose out to J.K. Simmons in Whiplash) I will say he is very good in his role, but not one of his best. I feel that the nomination here is based on past prowess. This is not the Duval of the Godfather (1972) or the Apostle (1997). If you haven't seen it check out the Apostle, it is Duval at his best I think. There is a great scene with him and Billy Bob that is amazing. So what derails the Judge from being one of the greats? ***************************Spoiler Alert********************************* One small very distracting plot element. After the funeral, Hank and his brothers go to a bar. He out verbally takes down an angry group of rednecks and wins over the affection of the hot young barmaid (Leighton Meester). She and Hank then make out like the ship is going down in a booth. Later in the film Hank meets up with his old flame Samantha (Vera Farmiga) where she informs him in a roundabout way that the girl at the bar may be HIS DAUGHTER. Then later she recants and says maybe she's his niece. This plot element is exponentially more distracting than say I don't know, knowing Leia was Luke's sister and re-watching them smooch to make Han jealous in Empire Strikes Back (1980). I know I'm a shameless nerd for that reference. Point being, incest is taboo and will halt a plot momentum in its tracks, having the audience shake their head in disgust. It was a minor plot twist that could have been omitted and kept a mid-level movie out of its mediocrity. The soundtrack as a side note does include two great little ditty's. Bon Iver's Holocene, and Willie Nelson's cover of Coldplay's the Scientist with his son Lucas. Overall this film is not without entertainment value. It is worth watching, but I say make it a Netfix or cable pick. I wouldn't put forth any money you're not already paying, or drive anywhere to see it. I could suggest dozens of other titles more worthy. If you enjoyed this review, please check out my blog at yourturntopick.com</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>Good cast, unbelievable story.</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>I was looking forward to seeing this movie. Some great actors, a promising story, so I was in for a treat.Well, to be honest, the acting was good (mostly) but the story itself was so wrong and unbelievable on so many levels, that it was ridiculous and pathetic at the same time.*** This review may contain spoilers *** First of all: OK, this son my not have liked his father (as it turned out, because of some punishment his father had given him when he was a teenager). Possible. And he never ever grew over it, despite the fact that he, as a child, in fact had been very close to his father. So he leaves home at the age of 20 and never ever comes back. But he obviously did love his mother, and liked his brothers. So why did he break up with them? Then, he gets a call (on his cell phone!) that his mother has died. It was his brother calling him. But if he had broken off all contact with his family, how did they know the number of his cell phone? Anyway, he heads home for the funeral. Despite the fact that he very much loves his mother, he does not shed a single tear when seeing her in her coffin. He looks at her dead body as if she is some "thing" and goes to the court house, where his father, the judge, is presiding a hearing. Really? Why would his father be working while his beloved wife, who just died, is laid out in a coffin at home, and people obviously can come in to bid her a last farewell (as all doors to the house seemed to be unlocked)? Then, those three brothers. Really? They are brothers? The oldest brother looked like 50 at least, Palmer looked like 40, and the youngest one looked like 25. In fact, at first I thought that the oldest brother was an uncle who was visiting with his slightly autistic son (the younger brother). They did not look like brothers, did not behave like brothers and were simply not believable as brothers.But we are still not there. The brothers decide to go out for the night and end up drinking in a bar. Palmer hits on the (very young) barmaid and ends up having sex with her, in a corner of the bar, for everyone to see. Are you kidding? And his two brothers are watching, drinking more beer and do not seem to care that their married brother is having sex with the waitress in plain sight? And all this just one day before the funeral of their mother? This waitress turns out to be the daughter of the older brother. This brother must have known (or at least suspected) that she was his daughter. Come on, small town Indiana, how many secrets can there be kept in a community where everyone knows everyone? So he in fact sees his brother having sex with his own daughter. But he does not seem to care a bit.And when Palmer later on learns that he in fact had sex with his niece ? he does not seem to care either. He is more upset by the fact that his former girlfriend slept with his brother (after he himself had left town!) than by the fact that he has been intimate with his own niece. Really? The whole story about the father (the judge) being administered chemotherapy in some lodge is highly improbable too. Come on! I myself have had chemotherapy (the last one just 6 months ago), and this has to be done in a hospital. Not only because of hygiene requirements, but also: something can go wrong or your body can react in an unexpected way, and in that case, direct medical attention is required. So no, you can not just hook up a bottle of chemodrugs to your body in some remote fishing cabin in the middle of nowhere.I could go on, there is much more, but I think you get the drift.The story did have potential. If they would have focused on the relation between the father and son and the court case, it could and would have been a better movie ? IMHO.</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>I expected more</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>The leading performances were nothing short of excellent. I won't be surprised if Duvall gets an Oscar nomination - his acting here is very impressive. Downey Jr. is also impressive, and so are D'Onofrio and Strong. All in all, the cast is great and if you watch the movie specifically for its acting, you won't be disappointed.Kaminski's camera work is also award worthy. This guy is one of the best cinematographers in the business and although 'The Judge' is far from his best works, it still looks great.Dobkin's directing is serviceable, although it is far from great.The music is mediocre at best. I have some experience as a musician and a composer but I can barely remember any of it.The writing is just a bit better than the music, and that is the movie's biggest problem. At first glance it looks well written but that is only because the actors are giving their best, and then some. As a drama about a dysfunctional family it works. It isn't nearly as great as it could have been but it works. As a courthouse drama it falls flatter than a pancake. Right on its face, I might add. The only thing it manages to show is that the American justice system is about as useful as... something completely useless. Given Downey's last line, I think this is exactly what the people who made the movie didn't want to show. I don't want to spoil it but what happens to Duvall's character is a direct consequence of his own stupidity. Not pride, although the writers probably thought they made his pride the main reason for what happened. Just stupidity. And that is not the only problem. As the movie goes on, you get to see or hear about some really impossible stuff - one of the central points of the drama involves such a huge conflict of interests... It just can't happen in real life and it sends the movie's realism down the drain. Honestly, it felt like the writers had drawn all of their inspiration from some TV-show rather than doing some actual research on the matter. There is the same rhetoric we have seen in all courthouse drama movies. Again, what we saw gave me the impression that all American lawyers are taking extensive acting lessons. And there is exactly the same - and rather forced - dramatic situation of the jury telling their decision we see in every movie with a lawyer as a main character. There are also a few intentionally comedic scenes that just do not fit the overly serious tone of the movie.Overall, the movie is very watchable. But that is all thanks to the actors and the cinematographer. The writing is forced, predictable and unrealistic, and it is far from the best in the genre.</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>Unoriginal film, but with great acting throughout</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>The Judge: When I first saw the trailer for The Judge, I thought "I have seen this kind of movie before. Why should I see it again?" I decided to see it for the same reason most people ended up seeing it, because of Robert Downey Jr. and Robert Duvall. They were the difference makers in this film. Without them, The Judge would have been a total failure. The only thing getting the audience past the clichés and generic plot we have all seen before is the amazing performances by Robert Downey Jr. and Robert Duvall. The fire and ice relationship between the father and son is nothing new and I can probably recall five movies in the past two years that have the same aspect in their movies. Luckily, its the first time they did it with these actors. Also the director goes way over the top when it comes to the subliminal messages it uses. For example, when the sun comes out, everything is resolved, but during a tornado, everything is chaotic. This technique is commonly used, so I cannot blame the director too much.If you have not gotten a feel of my opinion for the plot yet, I nicely say "it could have been better." Do not get me wrong, just because it is clichéd and predictable plot does not mean it is not enjoyable. I did enjoy The Judge. Still, it could have been better. The reason I enjoyed the film was almost entirely because of acting, but I also appreciated the shots. For this particular film, I seem to notice the high quality of cinematography. The cinematography really gave the audience a realistic view of how everything should look. Now we come to the acting. I firmly believe that no one could have done a better job than Robert Downey Jr. and Robert Duvall. Without them, this movie would have been way below average. The chemistry Robert Downey Jr. and Robert Duvall had on-screen was remarkable. If no one ever knew them before, they could have convinced that person they were father and son. This is not a typical father-son relationship either, it has to be in the top ten most-dysfunctional father-son relationships ever. It really reminded me so much about how a family is when a father and son are too much alike. This is why these actors are so good. Most everyone will like this movie, even if it is slightly forgettable. If you are fans of Robert Downey Jr. or Robert Duvall, I recommend you see this film in theaters. For more of my reviews, go to my website at reviewsbywest.com</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>A heartwarming courtroom/family drama.</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>The Judge is the story of Hank Palmer(Robert Downey Jr.) &amp; his family. Hank is a successful lawyer. After a terrible tragedy occurs in the family, he has to return to his home town. Here, he reunites with his family. His father Joseph Palmer(Robert Duvall), is a reputed judge in the town. Joseph Palmer is accused of murder. Now, it is up to Hank to defend his father in court. The Judge is a brilliant movie. Let me make things clear. The Judge is not just a courtroom drama. While the case is happening in the court, Hank reconnects with his brothers &amp; friends in his home town. The Judge is also about a father-son relationship that has undergone a lot of tension over the years. The movie is about how Hank &amp; Joseph try to rebuild that relationship. The courtroom aspect of the film makes it interesting. However, the family aspect of the film makes it emotionally satisfying. Don't get me wrong. There is a substantial amount of comedic scenes in the movie. The highlight of the film is the performances. Robert Downey Jr. is spectacular as Hank Palmer. Although Downey Jr.'s character is kind of the same old sarcastic jerk he always plays, there are many emotional scenes where Downey Jr. shows us the depth of his acting ability. Robert Duvall is outstanding as Joseph Palmer. Duvall has portrayed Joseph's anger &amp; vulnerability, effortlessly. Vera Farmiga is great as Samantha Powell. Billy Bob Thornton is brilliant as Dwight Dickham. Vincent D'Onofrio is incredible as Glen Palmer. Jeremy Strong is incredible as Dale Palmer. Dax Shepherd, Leighton Meester, Ken Howard, Emma Tremblay, Balthazar Getty &amp; David Krumholtz are awesome as C.P. Kennedy, Carla Powell, Judge Warren, Lauren Palmer, Deputy Hansen &amp; Mike Kattan respectively. You cannot miss The Judge. One of the best dramas I've seen in a long time.</fullReview>

		<username>GoneWithTheTwins</username>
		<score>-1.0</score>
		<shortDescription>The film isn't really about the trial.</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Hotshot Illinois lawyer Henry "Hank" Palmer (Robert Downey Jr.) is assured, sarcastic, and completely insensitive ? a character Downey Jr. has had plenty of experience portraying. "Innocent people can't afford me," he insists, possessing absolutely no qualms about liberating corrupt, law breaking citizens. Just as he's about to aid in a clearly guilty client's indemnification, Hank receives a phone call that secures him a continuance: his mother has passed away.Forced to return to his hometown of Carlinville, Indiana, a city where nothing ever changes, Hank meets up with his moderately alienated brothers and his completely estranged father, Judge Joseph Palmer (Robert Duvall) ? who barely makes eye contact with his distant son. The stern, decades-serving adjudicator firmly believes in his moral and ethical superiority; but that conviction is put to the test when he's accused of the first degree murder of Marcus Blackwell, a recently released killer previously sentenced to a 20 year bid by none other than Judge Palmer. With substantial evidence and a claimed lapse of memory during the alleged incident (a car accident), the elderly judge reluctantly relies on Hank to defend him in a case that could shatter his legacy and see him incarcerated for life.The film isn't really about the trial. Instead, it's a story of a painful past, old wounds, and the potential for redemption and reconciliation. Flashbacks of a happy childhood are envisaged to coax the audience into caring about a bitter old man who doesn't want help from his long-since-vindicated son, and who certainly doesn't deserve the attention. Hank himself isn't a signally likable character, but as everything goes wrong in his life (other than his vast wealth), starting with the revealing of a looming divorce, possible separation from his impossibly cute daughter (Emma Tremblay), and the death of his mother, he becomes the underdog to side with.The supporting cast is a stellar assemblage of notable character actors, each offering a level of entertainment to compensate for lingering moments of overly sentimental reminiscence. Vera Farmiga is the former flame, still harboring feelings of love and resentment for being left behind, while Leighton Meester is the seductive adult daughter; Billy Bob Thornton is the slick, seasoned prosecutor out to ensure justice; Vincent D'Onofrio and Jeremy Strong are the Palmer brothers, one a man living out a life of tragically lost athletic capability, the other a well-intentioned but mentally deficient, novice photographer, respectively; and Dax Shepard is the hopelessly inexperienced defense attorney destined to lose the case unless succored by someone who knows how to craftily manipulate the law. These roles alternate between comic relief and sources of extra familial drama (a clan likened to a Picasso painting), highlighted by a farcical jury selection process and occasional opportunities for moving remembrances. But with an overlong running time, the less affecting moments laboriously weigh down the pacing.The dialogue is awash with glib remarks and biting cynicism, making the picture strangely resemble the recent comedy "This Is Where I Leave You," which contained numerous coincidental similarities, including the death of a parent, a return to a small hometown, a reunion with a former lover, the correction of disaffection, the trading of stories of yore, and the revival of an unpleasant past. Although it spruces up plodding scenes of routines and unexceptional happenings, the discourse also frequently intrudes on serious settings to further distance the premise from the momentary glimpses of greatness that recall "Anatomy of a Murder," "The Verdict," or "?And Justice for All." It's a film of performances, not story, which always favors melodrama over thrills and tearful contemplation over taut courtroom battles.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<shortDescription>Robert Downey Jr. and Robert Duvall capture something beautiful in "The Judge"</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>"The Judge" is a failure in a lot of ways. It takes on too many stories at once and leaves most of them hanging?with no end in sight. The movie tries too hard to win an Oscar, and it's loosely held together by a fantastic father-son relationship with two star actors: Robert Duvall and Robert Downey Jr.The movie starts with Robert Downey Jr. pissing on a lawyer and ends with him looking at a chair. It involves an incestuous drunken night at a bar, a terribly outdated concert t-shirt, an awkward family and a whole lot of courtroom shenanigans with no real legal basis.The most memorable moment in the film is a scene in which Robert Duvall has explosive diarrhea all over the bathroom and the audience has the opportunity (displeasure?) of watching Robert Downey Jr. rinse said diarrhea off of his father. You could say that this scene didn't need to happen, and probably shouldn't have.But that would be missing the point. Yes, the movie has a lot of quirks. Yes, it's awkward and frustrating and leaves you with so many questions you feel like your head is going to explode. But that's the point. The film is all about the awkward, tense relationship between a hard-ass father dying of cancer and at risk of going to jail, and his not so straight-edge son. All that other stuff is just background noise to divert your attention from an on-screen relationship that could make even the most reserved man cry. The emotional appeal is what it's really all about. The other stuff?the renewed relationship with a past lover (and brief escapade with her daughter), the impending divorce and the strain that puts on a little girl, and perhaps most significantly, Robert Downey Jr. playing his typical role: a rich asshole?all provides comic relief from something that would be far too emotionally intense to sit through. And to the critics who argue that the film would fail if not for the star performances of Robert Duvall and Robert Downey Jr., you are completely off base. When the main focus of the film is to examine such an intense, strained relationship, then shouldn't the highlight of the film be the performances of those two actors? Yes?if not for those two performances the film would be awful. It would fail to hold the attention of the audience, and it certainly would not make the tears flow. But why is that a bad thing? Shouldn't we praise the movie for what it gets right? Because never before has a father and son relationship been captured so beautifully on screen.So should you go see two men named Robert hash out their differences on-screen? Absolutely?but don't forget to bring the tissues.</fullReview>

		<date>2014-10-14</date>
		<username>Wolfgang Koch</username>
		<score>0.8</score>
		<shortDescription>Deep drama, with periodic comic relief</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Some features of this movie seem hardly original: a legal eagle with slick tricks up his sleeve; a marriage on the rocks because one partner's mind is more on the career than on the family; a spark-laced encounter with an old high-school sweetheart right around that magical 20-year mark; and, finally, a dysfunctional family around which everything else revolves.Yes, there are clichés present in this movie. Their presence may, however, make it easier for us to follow both plot and character development; the clichés may be justifiable as devices to support a narrative that turns out to be much more complex, nuanced, and ambiguous as it unfolds, facet by facet.Front and center, of course, are father and son: Joseph Palmer (Robert Duvall) and Hank Palmer (Robert Downey Jr.). Other than both being officers of the court, they couldn't be more different from each other. Joseph is a principled judge with some of a holier-than-thou attitude, about which he feels right most of the time. Hank is a well-versed defense attorney with a somewhat unscrupulous finesse in making his case?as is necessary at times to get his client the most lenient sentence possible, if not an acquittal.And then Joseph suddenly stands accused of murder. He retains an attorney who really only moonlights as a lawyer, and who soon is in way over his head. Hank somewhat reluctantly takes it upon himself, then, to defend his father at trial.So?is Joseph guilty? When testifying in court, is he telling the truth? The movie slowly reveals and explores the demons Joseph is grappling with. One of these demons is a liability from Joseph's past and points to a motive for his alleged crime. Another of these demons is quite recent and might tarnish Joseph's legacy on the bench, regardless of the verdict.Meanwhile, Hank is grappling with demons of his own. These demons, dormant for a number of years, are now all the more virulent, now that Hank is on a forcedly prolonged stay in his hometown. Again, the movie takes time to uncover these demons in their intricacies.One of these demons is unrelenting in haunting both him and his older brother, Glen (Vincent D'Onofrio). Another has to do with his high-school sweetheart, Samantha (Vera Farmiga). Samantha, in turn, lives a lie she has been telling her daughter, Carla (Leighton Meester). This lie has a connection back to Glen, who isn't aware but would be if it weren't for Samantha's lie.So who, then, is to be "The Judge" of another? This movie illustrates how people have flaws, and therefore make mistakes that have long-term consequences. This movie shows that, even after the trial has ended, the consequences imposed by the system can be mitigated by compassion, the part of the human condition that atones for its critical flaws.And compassion is what the audience may legitimately have for both Joseph and Hank. Both are forever changed by confronting the unprecedented challenges in the turn of events. Neither Joseph nor Hank?nor anyone else, for that matter?can be readily filed under "good" or "evil." If all was not "well done," most was still "well-intended." It comes as no surprise that it is Robert Duvall's superb acting that facilitates this takeaway from the movie. The character of Joseph Palmer requires incredible stretches in the portrayal of a man with both sharp intellect and cognitive impairment, and both wiry stature and decrepit physique.So who, then, is to be "The Judge" of another? This movie shows different judgments passed in the court of law versus the court of public opinion. The former centers on certain actions, the latter centers on totality of character. And the way the story is told, in the end it is the court of public opinion that has it right.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<fullReview>Ugh, a simply dreadful hodge podge of courtroom thriller and family drama.Good actors like Robert Duvall, Robert Downey, Jr. and Vera Farmiga are asked to do something with a preposterous script that no one could save. Downey, Jr. chews scenery like it's his last day on Earth and delivers a terrible albeit energetic performance. He plays a hotshot attorney who's called back to his home town when his mom dies and ends up getting involved in a murder trial with his estranged father (Duvall) as the defendant. As they spend time together butting heads over the case, they also work out their mutual demons, with their big reconciliation scene actually occurring while Duvall is on the witness stand. Somehow, I just don't think a judge or prosecuting attorney would let the courtroom be used as a venue for the kind of telenovela plot exposition it's used for in this film.There are a hundred other subplots, including a ridiculous one involving Downey, Jr.'s ex-girlfriend and the questionable parentage of her college-age daughter. This entire plot strand could have been excised without changing the movie a whit, except that then we would have been denied the always-welcome presence of Vera Farmiga, even when she's stuck in a role she can't do a thing with. Billy Bob Thornton is on hand as the prosecuting attorney, and of course we have to have back story there too. This is the kind of courtroom film that calls for the prosecuting attorney to literally arch his eyebrows whenever it wants to remind us that he's the bad guy.With all of this overheated hoopla, the film is not surprisingly too long (almost two and a half hours!). My wife and I did have some fun venting our exasperation at how dumb the movie was and guessing at how much dumber it could possibly get. How this film managed to score a 7.5 user rating on IMDb ranks with the Kennedy assassination as one of the world's biggest unsolved mysteries.Grade: D</fullReview>

		<fullReview>Needing a break from software development, Ebola, and Kickstarter: I went to see the movie "The Judge" and left not sure whether I liked it or not.Reflecting on that, this is what I think happened:Many of use are familiar with changing something we are accustomed to, a healthier alternative, but change isn't easy. However, the anti-hero story is always rendered in a flash of time. The arrogance, drug use, womanizing, what-have-you always looks glamorous and fun. Realistically, if these behaviors were shown over a larger time-span, the results wouldn't be nearly as pleasant."The Judge" stars Robert Downey Jr, playing Hank Palmer, a prototypical anti-hero, a role Downey plays so well. However, it shows him over the course of growing up as a child into mid-life. The behavior that we've grown to like when depicted over a week or a month, is suddenly less appealing.It is realistic and healthy though. This is probably the most moral movie to have come out of Hollywood in years. A healthy change that will take some getting used to.</fullReview>

		<username>Likes_Ninjas90</username>
		<score>0.4</score>
		<shortDescription>Ultimately, the case presented relies on stale precedents we the jury have previously seen in more convincing fashion</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Does Hollywood do enough to encourage diversity? Robert Downey Jr.'s redemption from troubled drug addict to industry heavyweight overshadows the repetition of his high-energy performance registers. Not that Hollywood would be deeply concerned. This year Forbes ranked him as their Most Powerful Actor and listed tenth on the Celebrity 100 list. Forbes also cited the four Marvel films featuring his character Iron Man earning more than four billion dollars. His success still asks why he cannot diversify his performances and why more couldn't be made of The Judge, which marks a false start for his new production company Team Downey. Downey Jr. is fifty next year, a ripe old age for a superhero, which means he will enter unfamiliar territory by expanding his film choices. He and his wife Susan Downey are both producers on The Judge and their power in Hollywood should have enriched the star-studded casting with stronger talent. The cliché riddled script for example was rewritten twice, with the final version penned by Bill Dubuque, a first- time screenwriter. Similarly, the only memorable work by the film's director David Dobkin has been The Wedding Crashers, which is hilarious but hasn't prepared him to make a courtroom drama. The story of The Judge is homogenised from the outset, with the narrative telegraphing the predictable and old fashioned transformation of a big city Chicago lawyer who learns the value of family over money. Hank Palmer (Downey Jr.) is a hotshot lawyer with little time for his young daughter and whose punch line is: "Innocent people can't afford me". After his mother dies, he returns to his Indiana apple-pie hometown to visit his father Joseph (Robert Duvall), an ageing judge who is put on trial for a hit and run case. On the night of the accident, his father might have been back on the drink. Running at 140 minutes, the plot is overstuffed with melodramatic, Sparks-esque problems including: a life-threatening illness, a dysfunctional childhood, a lost love, a potentially estranged daughter, an intimidating lawyer and a family crisis. Not only are there too many elements in the script but they are encapsulated through safe, worn Hollywood staples, like male friendships and retrospective memories, with no new angles of their own. The father and son relationship is contrived because it is shoehorned into the courtroom scenes. Downey must represent his father when the town's nervous lawyer (Parenthood's Dax Shepard) isn't up to the task and as the tension spills into the trial the film's climax ridiculously doubles as a family therapy session.Similar to many mainstream films today, The Judge's adult characters are forced to relieve their childhoods, viewing them ludicrously as the linchpin of the problems in their current lives. While the court case alone could have made a solid 90 minute drama it is interrupted by family scenes infantilising Hank. He has two brothers: Glen (Vincent D'Onofrio), who was set to be a promising baseball player until a car accident injured his hand and Dale (Jeremy Strong), who is slightly challenged but crafty with a video camera. The home movie footage is a cornball excuse to include family footage and show how memories of the past resolve the current day crisis and enhance Hank's parenting skills. Does a person, particularly one well past midlife, become too old to be this sentimental and overly concerned about what happened in their childhood? Another thread further infantilising Hank is when he puts on a Metallica t-shirt and rides a bicycle, which coincides with his reunion with Samantha (Vera Farmiga). She is an old flame, now running a bar, and who has a daughter of her own. A pointless diversion about whether this is also Hank's grown up child, a disastrous thought after he kisses her, would be more suited to a straight comedy vehicle. Ultimately, the case presented relies on stale precedents we the jury have previously seen in more convincing fashion.On paper the cast looks exceptional but turn in serviceable performances. Each person is playing an archetype which does not enable them to fully embody the characters. Downey Jr. is typically charismatic, knowledgeable and confident as Hank but given his speech patterns and line deliveries are derivative of so many of his other characters we're left asking if there is anything more he can do in his sleep. He can still effortless spin one-liners and a few made me laugh, which lightens the film. It is a shame that the talents of Farmiga aren't fully utilised and she could have made a strong fist playing the opposing lawyer, which was given to Billy Bob Thornton who treats the role like he was instructed to be a brooding villain. These are problems typifying the weakness of the film: it feels like we are consciously aware this is a film and a construct because of the script's clichés and the general feel of the movie, where the tone slips away from the director as though he intended to make a broad comedy. No matter what Robert Duvall says, this film is not going to be as big as Apocalypse Now.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<shortDescription>Robert Downey Jr.'s Failed Attempt At Branching Out</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>The Judge commences with a usually cocksure Robert Downey Jr. as Chicago's successful lawyer, Hank Palmer, who's just one of those you despise?the one who gets the affluent guilty off scot-free. He has left his hometown of Indiana and family behind years ago in pursuit of a career before his mother's death finally reunites him with his family once again: a father who's a judge of righteousness and integrity (Robert Duvall); a phlegmatic and mentally slow brother (Jeremy Strong); and a more strong-willed, mindful brother (Vincent D'Onofrio). Many old friends and, especially, his father are angered by his sudden desertion those decades ago, and consequently, Hank feels alienated upon his return and deeply frustrated with these complicated dynamics. In a legal family, the concept of forgiveness seems to be elusive. That is until Hank's father?a long reigning and revered judge, mind you ?is accused of murder when a spatter of blood is found on his vehicle and the body of a murderer he strongly despised is discovered on the same road he drove down the previous night. Hank becomes wildly conflicted, unable to let go of the exhaustion and issues with his dad but also not buying a minute of these severe accusations. He forgets about Chicago and stays behind to defend his father in court, standing by the defendant in a criminal case yet again. Robert Downey Jr. and Robert Duvall are the obvious standouts here, and if any aspect of this film is worth the viewing, it's these two performances?competently acted through the various extreme emotions necessary as they struggle to coexist in the same house and tryingly fight through this case. Downey is frequently smarmy and smug to no surprise, but he's additionally allowed to effectively convey plenty of pensiveness and dejection. In the meantime, several distressing scenes show Duvall at his absolute physically worst as a dying man of cancer? crawling for dear life, vomiting, (realistically) defecating in his underwear as his son despairingly holds on. At the same time, there's some talent in the cast that are either wasted or honestly unnecessary. I'm not sure Vera Farmiga's role, Samantha Powell, was meant to be anything but the standard romantic interest/emotional support during a predicament that Hollywood needs to implement into their scripts for the sake of a female character's inclusion. After witnessing Gone Girl, the amazing and rich female character that is Amy only materializes just how awfully bland most productions make its females appear. The nostalgically romantic, sexy scenes between Downey and Farmiga offered very little, and alas extended the already-protracted runtime with its garrulous nature. On a more positive note, the impressively stark black-and-grey colors and theatrical lighting of Janusz Kaminksi's cinematography, allowing for the true vibrancy of a courtroom's hue, elevates the story beyond something that could've easily been reserved for the Lifetime channel. One of my favorite composers, Thomas Newman, inserts his typical elegiac, stirring score except that it seems transparently manipulative here, unfortunately. Every moment of overt sentimentality is accompanied by a quiet, weeping piano.Emotionally explosive scenes of compelling dialogue are occasionally dispersed throughout this otherwise painfully overlong, syrupy melodrama. You can expect an abundance of crying and yelling, as well as overdone family drama on the side that's all too predictably resolved. In the end, The Judge isn't the best next project for Downey in his attempt to branch out from superhero movies.</fullReview>

		<username>Michael_Elliott</username>
		<score>-1.0</score>
		<shortDescription>Great Performances but Some Real Flaws</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>The Judge (2014) *** (out of 4) Hot shot lawyer Hank Palmer (Robert Downey, Jr.) returns to his small hometown for the funeral of his mother. The short time he's there he realizes why his relationship to his father (Robert Duvall), the town's judge, was always on shaky ground but soon he is forced to defend him when the judge is accused of murdering a man he sentenced to prison twenty years earlier.THE JUDGE really could have been something terrific but it just feels like there was some sort of tinkering with the story to make it more mainstream. Obviously, I have no way of knowing what was going on during the filmmaking but all of the elements are here for a terrific, dark drama but it seems someone thought that they needed to add some really horrible comedy in hopes to make the film more marketable. It's really too bad that the flaws here are so bad or else THE JUDGE really could have been one of the best movies of the year since it does feature one of the best casts of the year.I will speak of the flaws first. The biggest is the fact that there's some horrid comedy thrown in for some reason. Again, I can only guess that someone at the studio thought the film needed to be a bit lighter so the comedy was added and I can assure you it helped nothing. There's a long subplot dealing with one of the brother's being mentally slow and why they tried to milk some comedy out of this is just beyond me. Another problem is some really over-dramatic sentimentality that the film goes for and especially towards the end. The movie is quite predictable, which is certainly another flaw but the added comedy and sugar was just way too much.With that said, there's still a very good film lurking here. I think the story itself was quite good as it really raised some ethical questions and I liked the fact that the judge was thinking of his honor, even after death. I thought that aspect of the story was pretty fascinating and I also thought the actual family drama was extremely good. I liked the relationship battle between the judge and his hot shot son but the supporting relationships were also good. These include the lawyers relationship to his brother (Vincent D'Onofrio) as well as an ex-girlfriend (Vera Farmiga). There are a few subplots that aren't as explored as they should have been, including the marriage crisis but overall the story works and makes for some fine drama.The real reason to see the film are for its wonderful performances with Duvall easily stealing the picture as the hard-headed judge. I thought Duvall really got to shine in a pretty complex and deep role that allowed him to show off his various talents. I thought ever aspect of the character was nailed by the veteran who I'm sure was excited to take on such a role. Downey, playing that smart guy everyone loves, manages to do that quite nicely but where he really shines is in the quieter, more personal moments with Duvall. Farmiga and D'Onofrio are both extremely good as is Billy Bob Thornton in his supporting role as the prosecutor.THE JUDGE is certainly a very flawed movie with some really stupid mistakes but there's no question that it has a very good story and some great acting. Put those two together and you've got a film that's certainly worth watching, although, in the end, you can't help but be upset that the flaws were as bad as they were.</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>Brilliant performances ...</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>"The law is the only thing capable of making people equal" Hank Palmer(Robert Downey Jr.) is a shrewd and successful lawyer with a smooth tongue. A chatterbox that overwhelms you with its arguments and you can't get in a word edge-ways. He uses arguments and counter-arguments in such a natural way, as if he's ordering something to eat at a sandwich bar. Therefore for some he's an insufferable person who does not mince his words. My favorite scene is the confrontation with some drunk local figures in a local bar where he and his two brothers Glen (Vincent D'Onofrio) and Dale (Jeremy Strong) chat about lost times. The way he gives those windbags tit for tat and knows how to silence them,is a perfect representation of who the person Hank Palmer is. This piece reminded me a bit of Matt Damon in "Good Will Hunting" who puts a group of university students in their place. Slyly I enjoy those moments. I know, schadenfreude is not a nice virtue.Because of family circumstances, Hank must return to his hometown which he turned his back on years ago. The funeral of his mother confronts him with his past. There are his brother Glen, who once stood at the beginning of a promising career as a professional baseball player, and his other mentally handicapped younger brother Dale, who has a passion for filming and usually carries a camera to capture all kinds of scenes. And then there's his father Joseph Palmer (Robert Duvall) who's invariably called "Judge" by everyone. A bitter man who can look back at an impressive career as a judge in the town of Carlinville and who's more concerned with his legacy as a righteous judge than his current miserable situation. The next day after the three brothers went on a night out, Mark Blackwell is found dead and his blood is found on the grill of Judge's car. Hank's father can't remember anything and pleads not guilty. Were it not, that years ago he made the error to give this Mark Blackwell a light imprisonment who after being released drowned a 16-year-old girl, there probably still would be some doubt. But this fact does give Judge a decisive motive for murder with hit and run."The Judge" is not a typical courtroom drama as we've seen frequently. It's a subtle made family drama with the legal dispute as a side issue and the emphasis on the family joust. Unresolved resentment and mistakes made in the past, played an important role. The whole is explained in detail in a brilliant and subtle way and time has been taken to outline the whole family situation, based on conversations and fragments filmed by Dale. And despite the significant playing time of 140 minutes, you won't get the feeling that you have to drag yourself through it. An intriguing and fascinating family sketch that sometimes tends to become melodramatic (culminating in the confrontation in the basement during a suddenly emerging tornado).It was to be expected that the confrontation between Downey Jr. and Duvall would guarantee some acting from the top shelf. A brilliant performance by Duvall as the stubborn paterfamilias, who nevertheless expresses some human feelings as his granddaughter comes to visit (incidentally also an admirable role played by Emma Tremblay). Downey can be arrogant. He demonstrated this already in "Iron Man". The difference is that this bugged me in "Iron Man", whereas it fits perfectly here. And definitely worth mentioning are the two emotional (and sometimes funny) renditions of D'Onofrio and Strong. Vera Farmiga ("The Conjuring"), as the ex-girlfriend of Hank, we see trotting along and merely serves as a distraction of the clash of arms between the two Palmer-clan members."The Judge" is a pleasure to watch when it comes to the acting, but ultimately it's still just a typical family drama with a court case as a side topic. For the rest, it's crammed with every imaginable emotive subject such as death, illness and an unwavering feud. The priority is the father-son relationship with some story-lines wrapped around it. Ultimately these stories have nothing to do with the core of the matter and only serve to incorporate frequent used clichés so it's guaranteed you'll use a handkerchief to wipe away a tear.More reviews here : http://opinion-as-a-moviefreak.blogspot.be</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>Justice for the judge</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>The Judge wants to be a heavyweight legal drama mixing family turmoil and ghosts of the pasts in a small Indiana town.Robert Downey Jr who actually is looking like a man approaching his 50s is a hot shot defence lawyer in Chicago. He defends guilty clients and gets them off using chicanery and is paid handsomely for his skills. As he says himself. 'Innocent people cannot afford him.'The film starts very economically. Within a few scenes you find out that Downey is a no nonsense lawyer, wealthy, that he has an estranged wife, he hates his small town roots, has had a rough relationship with his father and even get an idea of what type of person his father is, The Judge of the title.It is a shame then the rest of the film drags like a southern potboiler melodrama even though Indiana is a Midwestern state. Downey has two brothers who he gets along with just fine, one of them being disabled. He obviously loved his mother but you sense that there was friction in the past and the film lays clues that are rather easy to pick up on such as the older brother having a promising baseball career derailed because of injuring his hand in a car wreck. I wonder who might had been driving the car!Then there is the rekindling of a former romance who also happens to have a twenty-something daughter which might be the result of a past dalliance, complicated by the fact he was also canoodling early in the film with this young dazzler!Of course things come to a turn when the Judge is accused of murdering a former convict and Downey takes his case on. Its the courtroom scenes that fail to convince. I will credit the writers to follow an unconventional route with the court plot but surely an experienced Judge will not go to a novice lawyer first to defend him when he has possible the best defence lawyer as a son. Of course the novice is so useless that the Judge would had been better off defending himself even with his increased forgetfulness.The film was too long, even contrived. I just did not buy the murder plot and the Judge's approach to the trial given that he has been a Judge for decades he was acting like a wide eyed defendant hoping justice would ride to his rescue.There are good performances from the main actors who blend well together but the story does not hang well.</fullReview>

		<fullReview>Before I provide my final verdict for "The Judge", I must confess that I did not think Director David Dobkin had it in him to direct a serious drama. Dobkin is known for directing such comedies as "Wedding Crashers" and "The Change Up"; while they are fun flicks, they are not exactly challenging to the cerebral cortex."The Judge" is not perfect, it does have some monotonous scenes that should have been sentenced back to the editing room. But nevertheless, Dobkin did hold court pretty moderately in his orchestration of "The Judge". The movie stars Robert Downey Jr. as Chicago hotshot lawyer Hank Palmer. Hank goes back to his small hometown in Indiana for his mother's funeral. Hank's father is the town judge, Joseph Palmer; portrayed by the great Robert Duvall. Hank and Joseph have had a rocky relationship for years. When unexpectedly Joseph is arrested for murder, Hank decides to represent him even though daddy judge objects to that at first. Vincent D'Onorfio plays Hank's family-man brother Glen, and Jeremy Strong plays Hank's youngest brother Dale who has brain damage due to an accident that happened years ago with Hank being behind the wheel. The lovely Vera Farmiga also joins in the jury of supporting acting here portraying Hank's high school sweetheart Samantha. Billy Bob Thornton also makes a showing as the hard-knock prosecuting attorney Dwight Dickham, whose hard edge ways pretty much make him the first syllable of the character's last name. The muscle behind "The Judge" is most definitely the outstanding supporting performance from Robert Duvall, and the solid leading work from Robert Downey Jr. "The Judge" is guilty of a very few filmmaking flaws, but in no way would I object for anyone to view "The Judge", and let you judge it for yourself**** Good</fullReview>

		<fullReview>'The Judge', though clichéd &amp; a bit too long, is yet An Engaging Father-Son Story with Brilliant Performances! It isn't something amazing, but definitely among the better films we've seen in recent times. 'The Judge' Synopsis: Big city lawyer Hank Palmer returns to his childhood home where his father, the town's judge, is suspected of murder. Hank sets out to discover the truth and, along the way, reconnects with his estranged family.'The Judge', which is Written by Nick Schenk &amp; Bill Dubuque, is predictable &amp; even stretched beyond a point. 		<date>2014-11-04</date>
		<username>MisterWhiplash</username>
		<score>0.7</score>
		<shortDescription>top-shelf acting and a fairly smart script rise above (or with) conventions</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>The Judge comes not too soon after another film, also from Warner brothers, called This is Where I Leave You. Both movies have very similar conventional trappings (or just elements): a divorced man who has family issues has to go back to his small-town family when a parent dies, and there's tension immediately due to things from the past, and of course this all bubbles up over the course of the story. Where the Tropper adaptation tried to mix comedy and drama, to more or less effectiveness, The Judge is a straight-on melodrama but with the sense of humor one might want with Robert Downey Jr in the lead.This isn't a comedy, perhaps of the dark sort, though originally it might have been had Jack Nicholson been involved. With Robert Duvall playing the judge father to Downey's lawyer son, it's mostly serious. But this is a movie with BIG emotions going on in the midst of a murder trial - did Duvall strike a young nasty punk of a redneck down in the wake of his wife's passing - and the actors have to bring it, especially to a screenplay which isn't always subtle about the revelations and plot turns.But this is not to say that director David Dobkin doesn't do some service to showing things as painfully real - and maybe, in a sense then, kind of twistedly funny - like when the judge, with his stage 4 cancer running through him, has an incident in the bathroom. It's a hard scene to watch, and yet there's just a sliver of amusement, and it makes things that could be just sappy or over-dramatic (that is going over the bar for a scene's level) to being... human on a profound level.The murder case scenes in the courtroom, we've seen this stuff before. Hell, Downey Jr had a movie in the 80's, True Believer, where he was the younger lawyer to James Woods. Here, he's a full-blown star by now, and it's a given that he and Duvall command the screen when they come on. And since Downey is in almost every scene he has to play it well, but what's nice is how, in several scenes, he seems so relaxed in a way. Not that he isn't there to play the emotions asked, but he is so spot-on and comfortable with how to play it that it works better than if it had been another actor. Indeed the other players - Farmiga, Donofrio, Thornton - all have to get up to that level as well.Things that keep the film from being what it aspires to, a BIG movie of the fall season that can contend with the likes of Gone Girl and Fury as far as pulse-pounding dramatic movies that aren't simply for teenagers: some odd-quirky things, like the mentally challenged younger brother (he is a sweet presence in the story, it just still seems like a 'hook' of a sort to hang certain moments on, to spell things out for obvious comedic effect), and a sort of sub-plot involving Farmiga (again, similar to something we just saw in 'Leave You') that leads to a revelation that just detracts from the main storyline, which is much more engaging.If you're looking for something that has some strong dramatic bite - there hasn't been a John Grisham adaptation in a while, and this has the feel of his stronger stories, for example - and of course these actors delivering in three-dimensions, the BIG confrontations, the more awkward and yet harrowing moments where voices are kept normal but tension is high, and the few happy beats (near the end a scene on a small boat is really affecting), this is a good choice. The climax is also good too, in the sense of not being unrealistic about what has come before in the trial but still giving the audience something satisfying. It's not great, but the movie has its cake and eats it.</fullReview>

His presence is the best thing about the film, and creates a reason to be remembered more than Duvall. 		<username>TheSquiss</username>
		<score>0.6</score>
		<shortDescription>Duvall &amp; Downey Jnr's courtroom drama of family feuds. More meat than a Grisham</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>The Judge is one of those films they sneak out between the summer blockbusters and the worthy, winter, winning hopefuls; it is too small, too niche to go head to head with the sequels and blockbusters but not quite edgy enough to be a serious contender for gold when we get down to the nitty gritty of the awards season.However, David Dobkin's legal drama is a solid enough flick with plenty to hold the attention and some engaging performances to while away the two hour plus running time. It may not have the tension of courtroom heavyweight A Few Good Men or the gravitas of JFK, but The Judge achieves much as a John-Grisham-with-more-meat production.Hotshot Chicago lawyer, Hank Palmer (Robert Downey Jnr), reluctantly returns to his small town, family home when his mother dies. But whereas he makes decent ripples in the city's legal pond, in the sleepy town of Carlinville, Indiana, it is his estranged, outwardly hostile father, Judge Joseph Palmer (Robert Duvall) who is the big fish. Eager to see the funeral through and leg it back to Chicago post-haste, Hank is forced to stay when the judge is accused of murdering a former defendant with his car. Despite their mutual dislike for each other, Hank must defend the judge and scrape through the muck and bitterness to the truth of the case and their own family secrets.Like August: Osage County, The Judge is a movie about families tolerating and coming to terms with each other's foibles and reluctantly burying the hatchet, ideally not in one anther's backs, against the backdrop of a forced gathering. The courtroom drama serves to spice up the proceedings and is a great MacGuffin, but the detail is in the relationship between father and son.There are no startling revelations that rock us, just a steady evolution of time and patience as both men air grievances and rail against each other's obstinate manner. Duvall and Downey Jnr make for excellent and watchable sparring partners. Both have been here before but not pitted against each other and this is a marvelous display of Hollywood royalty on fine form.The supporting characters are fluff to give context or a change of shape and pace to The Judge. Each subplot reinforces the complication of family life in its various forms and several of them could easily be the central theme (and frequently have been): brothers with a bitterness based on guilt and regret; long-lost girlfriend (Vera Farmiga as Samantha) who still holds a candle for him; philandering stud consumed by career who rediscovers the attraction of the simple things? The Judge is almost a patchwork quilt of ideas, but it works. It is an absorbing film of tenderness and honesty that frizzles with the electricity of family feuds and lightens the atmosphere with occasional, stonking one-liners courtesy of scribes Nick Schenk (Gran Torino) and Bill Dubuque.There are one or two blatantly staged moments (could the outcome of the bicycle ride be any more obvious?) and The Judge takes a half step away from reality in the culmination of the final courtroom scene, but that's forgivable given the outcome and aftermath.Dobkin, whose work as director to date hasn't thrown up anything deeper than Wedding Crashers and The Change-Up, has crafted an attention-grabbing film with a cast that does him great credit. I departed the screening buoyed by the experience and feeling fulfilled. A couple of days later the details are hazy (whereas we can all still quote lines from A Few Good Men) but it remains a positive experience that fades pleasantly and is worthy of a second viewing one day.For more reviews from The Squiss, subscribe to my blog and like the Facebook page.</fullReview>
	</review>
