		<username>Joe Chadowski</username>
		<score>0.7</score>
		<shortDescription>The most average movie I've ever seen</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>The Judge is the tale of Hotshot defense lawyer Hank Palmer (Iron Man) who has shut out his family and hometown from his life is then dragged back after the death of his mother. After paying his respects, and clashing with his family, especially his emotionally distance father, a judge (Robert Duvall), Hank high-tails it out of there. Only to be dragged BACK in when his father (accidently?) hits a kills a local trailer-trash scumbag. What really happened that night seems fuzzy, and Hanks defense is exactly what his father needs, but will they overcome their differences in time for court?The Judge tries too hard to be both dramatic and light-hearted. Robert Downey Jr.'s sarcastic, fast-talking', too-smart-for-his-own- good character is a synthesis of every post-drug rehab role he's ever played. Which adds some welcome hilarity to the story, but every joke, every punch line comes at the worst time possible; either in the midst of a dramatic scene, or just after it, corrupting The Judge with a jumpiness that disconnects you from the film. And despite the bloated runtime, the script isn't as dense as it should have been, and wastes time on useless tangents that are either ham-fisted in their delivery, or contribute nothing to the crux of the story. That said, production quality in the Judge is high. The photography is exceptional, much of the beauty coming from to the scenic town of Jackson, Indiana, but also Janusz Kaminski's utilization of contrasting colors and earth tones. Even Dobkin's overuse of lens flaring works to some degree. Pity, then that the script is as jagged and inconstant as the scenery is captivating. But despite the scripts inconsistent and spastic nature, occasionally it lingers on Robert Duvall long enough for him to show us how a legend does his thing. The rest of the cast does a decent enough job, but their underdeveloped characters and the darty script never give them a chance to fully flex their talent.The Judge is the most average movie I've ever seen; a good concept poorly executed, a film with a good message that tries to be too many things. It's heart is in the right place and it has a strong moral center, but it's let down by a lack of effort from those behind the camera. So the dictum goes, "if you try to please everybody, you'll end up pleasing nobody. The Judge will have its 5 seconds of fame during its time in theatres, then it will be forgotten, revived only years from now when it graces lists of films that are good, but you've never seen. Take that as you will.</fullReview>

		<username>The_Film_Cricket</username>
		<score>-1.0</score>
		<shortDescription>Two performances in an imperfect movie . . .</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>If you have any interest at all in the new legal thriller The Judge, then it is safe to say that you're probably drawn in by the casting. Here in the same film are Robert Downey, Jr. ? one of the best actors of his generation, and Robert Duvall ? the great lion of the American cinema. That's a powerhouse combination and it is a joy to report that neither disappoints, even when the movie itself falls by the wayside.The Judge resembles those John Grisham thrillers that were popular in the 90s before the public came to the realization that he was basically telling the same story over and over. This is not a Grisham thriller, it's actually better because it has more investment in the characters. Yes, it's about a big criminal trial but that's only a backdrop. It's just as effective as a human drama.At the film's center is Robert Downey, Jr. as Hank Palmer, a successful self-centered Chicago defense attorney who is so smug that his personal motto is "Innocent people can't afford me." On the cusp of a major case, Hank is called home to Carlinville, Indiana with the news that his mother has suddenly passed away.It's not hard to guess that in returning to his hometown he is not met with warm hugs and tea. Actually he's met with disdain and personal grudges, mostly from his extended family, a colorful bunch that is headed by Judge Joseph Warren (Duvall) a 42-year veteran trial judge whose personal style in court is based on common horse sense. Around town he's a local legend. As with everyone else, Hank and the old man have a prickly relationship and there's an emotional distance between them that you could drive an oil tanker through. The old man isn't just some crusty old goat, he's a reasonable man who is disappointed by some of the choices that Hank has made, coupled with the fact that he has just lost his wife of 50 years.Hank can't wait to get back to Chicago, but (Spoiler Alert!) he wakes up one morning and finds some damage done to his father's car. It seems that the judge ran over a man that he previously put in jail. Was it a grudge? Was it an accident? Was the Judge even there? The facts of the case are really just filler. The best parts of the movie deal with the emotional distance between Hank and the people he left behind in Carlinville. There is no love lost, but when the old man is in trouble, he's forced to deal with dark chapters in his life that he thought were closed forever.The great joy of The Judge is Robert Downey, Jr. whose performance reminds you of the reasons why he became a movie star in the first place. It's been a long time since we've seen him in a film outside of Iron Man and Sherlock Holmes (and based on his dance card for the next few years, it may be a while before we see it again). Here he's allowed to play a full-blooded character. There's a lot of Tony Stark in Hank Palmer, especially in the film's opening, but Downey surprises us by displaying a great deal of upfront vulnerability. Hank is a showman in court, but he's also a human being with a heart; a man who has made mistakes that fill him with bitterness and deep regret. Downey has proved that he can act with the power of his mouth, here he shows that he can do the same with the power of his heart.The performances make up for lags in the script. This is a deeply human drama, but often the drama seems to drag. Some scenes go on too long. There are too many characters characters and subplots, especially a needless, tacked-on romance between Hank and his old high school sweetheart (Vera Farmiga) that could have been excised from this film without affecting the main story at all.The film comes from David Dobkin, who made The Wedding Crashers a decade ago and has spent his time making dopey comedies like The Change Up, Mr. Woodcock and Fred Claus. Here he's trying his hand at something more serious, and while he doesn't completely succeed in the story department, you must admire the performance he's able to get from Downey and Duvall. He lets the characters breath and doesn't tighten them down by the demands of the plot. It's a good effort, it's a good movie. It's not great, but it's worth seeing.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<shortDescription>Critics are wrong. Powerful court room drama.</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Robert Downey, Jr. returns to the screen as Henry Palmer in The Judge, directed by David Dobkin. Henry is a successful Chicago defense attorney who is good at his job and provides well for his wife and grade-school aged daughter. The wife is bored because Henry works so hard and is seriously contemplating a divorce.When Henry learns that his mother has suddenly and unexpectedly passed away, he returns to the small Indiana town of his childhood. There he is reunited with his older brother, Glen (Vincent D'Onofrio), his younger, mentally challenged brother Dale (Jeremy Strong), and his estranged father, Judge Joseph Palmer (Robert Duvall). Glen was a baseball super star in his youth with a promising future that was cut short when he was involved in an automobile accident. Dale is a tender man who loves his brothers and his movie camera. Judge Palmer values his reputation in the community and has strong beliefs in right and wrong.Judge Palmer becomes a murder suspect when a low-life he had previously sentenced is hit and killed on his bicycle. Henry defends his father in court. The court room drama is tense and thrilling and Robert Downey, Jr. and Robert Duvall steal the show as old wounds are ripped apart. The chemistry between the two of them is palpable, believable, and spell binding. Despite a few scenes leaning sentimentally, the critics have been way too harsh on this gripping spellbinder that blends court room suspense with a father and a son trying to understand each other.Cinematographer Janusz Kaminski makes this film beautiful to watch with grand court houses, bucolic fields that seem to stretch forever, waterfalls, and a picture postcard small town. Robert Downey, Jr. delivers as the successful son with the emotionally absent father. Entertaining and engrossing.</fullReview>

		<date>2014-10-09</date>
		<username>Brent Hankins</username>
		<score>0.8</score>
		<shortDescription>A tangled mess of cliché-ridden story lines offset by riveting performances from Duvall and Downey.</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Since his first appearance as Tony Stark in 2008's Iron Man, Robert Downey Jr. has enjoyed a seemingly never-ending parade of roles that have allowed him to basically portray an exaggerated version of himself. Whether it's headlining the Sherlock Holmes franchise or popping in for a cameo in John Favreau's Chef, we always get the sense that Downey isn't so much acting as he is just channeling his innate charisma and energy into a different direction, and it becomes easy to forget that he's an acclaimed performer with a couple of Oscar nominations under his belt.If you're looking to be reminded of Downey's dramatic capabilities, then David Dobkin's The Judge should do the trick nicely. Downey stars as Hank Palmer, a big shot defense attorney who measures success by the price of his car and the size of his house (and his paycheck, as he quips to a colleague "innocent people can't afford me"). In denial about his crumbling marriage and barely able to connect with his daughter, Hank's life is further complicated by a phone call from the small Indiana town where he grew up: his mom has just passed away.Returning home for the funeral brings back plenty of memories, both good and bad, and reignites Hank's conflicted relationship with his father (Robert Duvall), a well-respected judge with over 40 years of service on the bench. Judge Palmer has little respect for his son's choice of career, and the two can barely manage to make eye contact when they're in the same room together, let alone carry on a civil conversation.That changes when Judge Palmer is arrested for his suspected involvement in a fatal hit-and-run accident, the victim of which has a past intricately linked to the entire Palmer clan. Hank immediately jumps to his father's defense, a move that Judge Palmer reluctantly accepts when it becomes clear that his young, inexperienced defense attorney will get eaten alive by special prosecutor Dwight Dickham (Billy Bob Thornton), who intends to make an example out of the judge.The sequences of events sets the stage for a riveting courtroom spectacle, and while The Judge delivers on that promise, it actually takes a backseat to the family drama that plays out in the foreground. Hank's attempts to make peace with his father are merely a single piece of the puzzle, as he also struggles to connect with his brothers (Vincent D'Onofrio and Jeremy Strong) and finds himself drawn to the high school sweetheart (Vera Farmiga) that he left behind. While some of this material works very well, much of it feels extraneous and does little more than slow the pacing of the film.The Judge also suffers from many of the tried and true clichés that plague films about disconnected families, but we're willing to forgive those shortcomings thanks to the strength of the performances, particularly Downey and Duvall. While the script is surprisingly humorous and allows Downey to showcase some of the quick-witted cynicism that has become his trademark, the best moments come when he and Duvall are allowed to truly go at it. Years of anger and resentment manifest in every confrontation, bubbling up to the surface until the two actors are exchanging the the equivalent of verbal haymakers, each delivered with more force than the last. At 141 minutes, The Judge overstays its welcome by about 20 minutes, made even more perplexing by the fact that the filmmakers chose to prattle onward well after a poignant moment that would have served as a much better ending than we were ultimately given. But truthfully, we don't care that much, just like we don't care about the confused, tangled mess of story lines that muddy the waters of the narrative. Those things aren't nearly as important as watching Downey give his best dramatic performance in nearly a decade, or being reminded precisely why Duvall is one of the most enduring screen performers of all time.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<shortDescription>A sappy melodramatic film with good performances</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>The Judge stars an all star cast featuring the likes of Robert Downey Jr., Robert DuVall, Ver Farmiga and Billy Bob Thorton and is as surprisingly bad as a film can get. This goes to show that star power doesn't always guarantee a great film. The plot follows Hank Palmer, a stereotypical hot shot lawyer played effortlessly by Downey as he begins to prep for the case of his life, defending his own father, played by Robert DuVall. While the story does sound something like Oscar bait, this film tries to do everything right for all the wrong reasons. Quite frankly, this film is slow, barely touching, but well acted. It seems that Robert Downey, Jr is stuck in a playboy role. He does this so good and so effortlessly that I am hard pressed to give him praise because we've seen this type of character from him countless times. While his performance is good, he can't seem to separate himself from the Tony Stark persona that he has come into within the past seven or so years. Robert DuVall is apparently the go-to actor nowadays for either grumpy old men or estranged fathers with the best advice, both typecast roles he exhibits in The Judge. Much like his co-star, DuVall plays this role effortlessly and has played this role effortlessly for the better part of two decades. Its become old and tired with every film he continues to put out. Despite seeing nothing new from the two, the performances from our leads are good and do keep the film going as best as it can. The real standouts here were the supporting actors. Billy Bob Thorton, Vera Farmiga and Vincent D'nofrio are all fantastic in this and never miss a beat. If there was ever a good enough reason to see The Judge it is for these small handful of performances that stand out and shine brighter than the leads. The plot to the film, while somewhat unique doesn't really pack the type of emotional punch you want it to. Yes, there are sad parts and "que the traditional sappy music" type scenes but these well acted scenes are overshadowed by the sheer redundancy of them. We can only see Hank ask his father a question only to get a snippy answer so many times before we start to question Hank's motives. Why even try? The film doesn't touch base on a clear cut reason to why other than the fact that he's family and he feels obligated to. It didn't touch me on the emotional level I wanted it to. It didn't make me want to call my father after, it didn't make me think at all. I will say that when I saw David Dobkins directed this film, I admired his courageousness to be able to go from a romping comedy such as Wedding Crashers to a film as heavy as this. Despite this big move from comedy to drama, it only showed that Dobkins should stick to comedies. At least he has proved he is VERY good at that. This was an after-thought type of film, one you watch and enjoy to an extent but never really feel as though you want to dissect it or even watch it again. Overall, I was thoroughly disappointed with The Judge as a whole. The film has a couple really brilliant performances namely Vera Farmiga and Billy Bob Thorton but other than these performances, The Judge is a disappointing film that never really taps into anything remotely powerful during its bloated running time.</fullReview>

		<username>emmueller95</username>
		<score>0.8</score>
		<shortDescription>The Judge is a Must See</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>The Judge is not merely a story about a son defending his father, who is on trial for murder, but a story about a son restoring his relationship with his father while also finding himself. The movie stars Robert Downey Jr., Robert Duvall, and Vera Farmiga. Hank Palmer (Downey Jr.) is a successful, Chicago lawyer from a small town, who left and never looked back, and if it's possible, never go back. Hank is arrogant and doesn't care if he is defending people who are obviously guilty. On top of this, he has a daughter and is in the early stages of divorce with his wife. Palmer must begrudgingly go back to his hometown of Carlinville, Indiana when he learns of his mother's death. There we find out he has a very estranged relationship with his father, who happens to be the local judge. While visiting, Hank's father, Judge Palmer (Duvall), is convicted of murdering a man he had sentenced to prison for 20 years and was recently released. Hank reluctantly agrees to represent his father, who in turn reluctantly let's his son represent him. This is not an easy case for Palmer. Throughout the movie, Hank and his father struggle to see eye to eye and to resolve their issues from the past. They both must get past their egos and pride. Hank struggles and just wants to get back to Chicago. But, while home, Hank also runs into a former girlfriend, Samantha Powell (Farmiga), who tries to help Hank get back to his roots. Hank struggles with this throughout the movie. One thing about The Judge, from a technical aspect, is it also uses lighting very well. When in Chicago as a lawyer, the use of three- point lighting makes this realm bright and feel almost fake. While in Carlinville, the use of natural and key-lighting is more predominant, making the scenes feel more realistic. This is also shown well in the respective courtrooms. In the Chicago courtroom, the use of three-point lighting makes it feel more cheery for Hank, as though he has the power here and he doesn't care about his clients. But in the Carlinville courtroom the use of low-key lighting makes the room darker and shows the seriousness of the situation, as though Hank feels he has no power, and the case actually means more to him than just a paycheck. The Judge is an excellent movie, with Downey Jr. doing what he does best: using sarcasm to make light of situations. Downey Jr. excels in the role of Hank Palmer and is becoming a modern icon. When he is in a film he seems to take over. His characters are similar, but he excels at them and does them so well, it doesn't matter. The Judge shows the struggles of a man who has to swallow his pride and try to cooperate with his father, while in the process of trying to rebuild that broken relationship and trying to get back to his roots, all the while dealing with different personal dilemmas along the way.</fullReview>

		<username>Jon Santana (spdr11)</username>
		<score>1.0</score>
		<shortDescription>One of the best Downey Jr, Roles ever</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>The Judge starring Robert Downey Jr. and Robert Duvall screened at the Mill Valley Film Festival before its theatrical release, where story writer and director David Dobkin was able to make an appearance for a Q&amp;A, which we have transcribed and included below our review. Superb, truly an instant classic and must-see for any film lover or actor; because it truly is a writer's film and an actor's film. . Hank Palmer (Robert Downey Jr.) is a fast-talking, win-at-all cost big-city criminal lawyer who travels back to his picturesque hometown of Carlinville, Indiana for his mother's funeral. But after his father Joseph Palmer (Robert Duvall), a well-known and respected judge in the small town, is accused of murdering a man he had once sentenced to time in prison; Hank stays back to defend his father in court. Things get more complicated when Dwight Dickham (Billy Bob Thornton), a man disgusted by Hank's morally questionable methods, steps in as the prosecutor. . While working to prove his father's innocence, Hank gets reacquainted with his brothers; Glen Palmer (Vincent D'Onofrio), a soft-spoken giant with a once-prominent shot at the Major Leagues; and Dale Palmer (Jeremy Strong), a child in all but age with an 8mm film habit. And long-ago girlfriend Samantha (Vera Farmiga) a self-sufficient small business owner. . Every single element of the film was superb, from the story and writing to the art direction and cinematography to the breathtaking performance of every single actor. And audience member in the Q&amp;A at the festival pointed out that the film feels as if it were based on a classical novel; a staple like To Kill A Mockingbird reimagined for a more contemporary audience. Shockingly, it's an original screenplay; a collaboration between David Dobkin and screenwriters Nick Schenk and Bill Dubuque.These scenes were made even more powerful with the level of talent present in this film. Robert Downey Jr. and Robert Duvall were simply amazing. The actors went somewhere they haven't gone before, no better example than the bathroom scene, serving as a perfect tipping point in the story and their relationship. But every major character had their moment in the spotlight. Vincent D'Onofrio, Jeremy Strong, Vera Farmiga and Billy Bob Thornton were great supporting characters and we're quickly becoming Leighton Meester fans after seeing her in Like Sunday Like Rain and now in this film, where her brief appearance is very memorable.Perfect balance of drama and humor. Plenty of laughs throughout, which all come from the honesty of the characters and their situations. Downy Jr.'s natural sarcasm and ability to make just about anything funny help too. The heavy humor didn't take away from the intense drama of the story at all. If anything, the funny moments made the dramatic ones even heavier.Every story element came together so well, with just enough of a resolution for every single subplot; without going too far with the 'ending' lasting forever. The very last shot of the film is just subtle enough to be artistic and beautiful, just telling enough to be a satisfying ending.SPOILER ALERT!!:This film would be a tough one to find the right ending for; one which will both satisfy the audience and teach the protagonist enough of a lesson. So many well-written films screw it up in the third act, but this one ended so perfectly. The noble man must sacrifice himself to be true to the morals he hopes to pass on. But his son must also be given a chance to make a difference, to use his talents for good.So many people are able to be virtuous, until something huge goes wrong. Then they're willing to do anything to make it go away. Downy Jr.'s character didn't understand why his father wasn't willing to do anything to stay out of prison. He had to learn that morals are just for show until you're willing to sacrifice for them. If you abandon them when it finally matters, they're nothing. Sacrifice makes a more lasting impact on others, even in a small town it's an impact worth sacrificing for.His father cared deeply about his impact on others, and on the world he would one day leave behind. His martyrdom was a message to all witnesses, that his virtues of honesty and justice truly matter. And through honesty and the justice system, he was still able to get out long enough to spend his final hours with his son, fishing on a lake and recalling favorite memories. This is one of those few films that deserves nothing less than a 10/10 rating.</fullReview>

It began as a sad story about a successful lawyer returning to his hometown (Downey Jr.)after his mother's death, to a place with so many bad memories but more importantly to a place where his father lives and works, a father he hasn't spoken to for many years. But in the end the movie was so much more than that.The acting is excellent. Robert Duvall is truly wonderful here as the lawyer's father and THE judge. I do believe this could be his Oscar worthy performance.As for Robert Downey Jr. I thought he was a very good comedian (which he gets to practice in this movie as well) but he is so much more. I truly have more appreciation for the man's acting capabilities. Also, an Oscar worthy performance.The messages are many. I found that the movie tried, and succeeded with flying colors, to mash up together a murder story and a story about family and how to break the chains and walk out of their shadow and ultimately a story about life, death and revenge.The punchlines come when you least expect them and for a drama movie there are a lot of funny lines, perfectly given by the two lead men, who literally take the movie on their shoulders and carry it on until the finale.Speaking of finale, you keep guessing what actually happened, as the director made sure we watched the movie from the judge's point of view. We, like the judge, are clueless as to what really happened and if we (the judge) did really commit the murder. The shots are beautiful and especially the court scenes are both shot and played to perfection. The underline messages are so much more (which I can't go into detail) but I give high praise to Robert Duvall's acting. I highly recommend this movie. I think the buzz is just beginning and I expect the movie to be at least on 6-7 different categories in the Academy Awards Ceremony (including Best Director, best Leading actor, best screenplay and best cinematography). If you are a fan of great story telling, wonderful acting and a close to perfect directing then this is the movie for you.WATCH IT AT ALL COSTS</fullReview>

		<fullReview>Soo, was initially hesitant as well as excited to watch this film. Hesitant after reading some of the unfavorable critic reviews labeling the film as 'cliched','formula', simply praising the performances of Downey Jnr &amp; Duvall. Excited because well, I'm a fan of courtroom dramas &amp; when you have epic actors like Downey Jnr &amp; Duvall in a film together, I really don't need another reason to see the filmAfter watching The Judge, I can clearly say all my preconceptions and assumptions got blown out of the window, along with the unfound negative criticism it received. Contrary to belief the film is not simply a formula films, Yes the story of a cut throat city lawyer turning ethical to save his dad from prison, may appear to be a bit seen-their-done-that. This is simply the topping of the film. The film is richly layered with a plethora of stories, genres &amp; relationships, generously intertwined into this story. The delicate way the film handles the complex strained relationship between son &amp; his father, as well as his siblings is one of the finest &amp; most honest portrayal I've seen (and I've probably watched almost every estranged son-father film made). Most of all its an honest yet entertaining film. Its brave enough to not fall into a Disney fairytale ending, of many courtroom and dysfunctional family film. It has the guts to stay true to the story, its characters &amp; the reality of when you have a family broken in many ways trying to function. The protagonist doesn't run off with his high school sweet heart into the sunset, nor does he manage to pull of a courtroom miracle to stop his father going to prison with his incredible lawyer skills, nor does his father doesn't jump into his arms at the end. YET it is still is a film which leaves you with hope &amp; redemption. How David Dobkin created a film of this nature is truly a miracle. Its not a film which relies on its stellar cast to keep it running This is genuinely a great film in all aspects, screenplay, story, acting &amp; direction. Like myself you may just initially see this film purely to see Downey Jnr &amp; Duvall, but I warn you, you will end up seeing a great film</fullReview>

		<date>2014-10-25</date>
		<username>Argemaluco</username>
		<score>0.8</score>
		<shortDescription>The Judge is an interesting and quite entertaining film</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>It's not an excessively popular subject, but we occasionally see films about the "prodigal son (or daughter)" who lives in the big city and returns to his/her town, where he/she faces a clash of ideologies while learning something about the values and traditions those conservative communities still have. That formula is sometimes presented as a comedy, sometimes as a drama, sometimes as a romance, and sometimes all together, like in the film The Judge, whose well built screenplay offers some laughs, a bit of romance and, specially, the family drama between a son and a father who never understood each other, until the tragedy forces them to cooperate... and maybe discover virtues they weren't willing to recognize. And an actor who can keep that delicate balance drama and comedy is definitely Robert Downey Jr., who is able to express deep emotions with a pleasant lightness which doesn't contradict the sincerity of his character's feelings. His excellent performance in The Judge is enough reason to see this movie... but there's much more. In the complicated role of the father, we have Robert Duvall, who expertly handles his scenes without any effort, while perfectly complementing Downey Jr.'s performance. And in the supporting cast, we also have solid performances from Vera Farmiga, Leighton Meester, Vincent D'Onofrio, Billy Bob Thornton, Jeremy Strong and Dax Shepard. The Judge is a film in which everything fits into its place, without aspiring to go beyond the minimum narrative requirement. The screenplay touches the obligatory bases from family dramas and legal thrillers; the actors make a perfect work in their roles; and director David Dobkin (whose previous filmography is mediocre at best -The Change-Up- and atrocious at worst -Fred Claus-) drives the film with an austere measure, without wasting his time into excessive style or visual flourish. In other words, there isn't too much ambition or novelty in The Judge, but there's no need to have them when the pieces work in a good harmony, conjugating with each other in order to create an entertaining, satisfactory and emotive experience (even though it's occasionally manipulative and a bit longer than it should). The legal aspect brings suspense and structure, but the most important elements of the film are the family conflict and the interaction between the actors. So, I wouldn't consider The Judge an excellent film, but I recommend it as a solid combination of family drama and legal thriller which inspires a bit, entertains pretty much and offers brilliant performances from the whole cast.</fullReview>
	</review>

Try not to be distracted by your own memories of family, and let them come and linger for your post-movie enjoyment. This review is unsolicited and straight from my heart. The world is a better place tonight because of this movie, and I can think of no higher honor that can be sought. Knox D. Alford, III</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>A good break from seeing Robert Downey Jr. as a superhero</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Facing a divorce, a great yet not so honest city lawyer Hank goes back to his home town in Indiana to attend his mother's funeral. He meets his father Joseph, an honest judge of the town who have always been an opposition to his ways and opinions. After leaving with anger over an argument about the divorce, Hank returns again as his brother tells him his father is charged with manslaughter with his car. The victim is a Mark Blackwell whom Joseph tried once. Hank easily sets his father out of interrogation but further investigation really makes Joseph a suspect. For the preliminary hearing, Joseph chooses his lawyer, an honest but seemingly incompetent man to defend him, whom Hank repeatedly harass over which. The case goes on to trial and the lawyer stepped down admitting his lack of experience. Being formal, Hank offers his father a 'contract' on Hank being his counsel. Yet there are still instances where Joseph insists on using honest ways, contradicting Hank's cunning ways of the loopholes. Hank's own investigations find that Joseph is undergoing a medication that can cause some degree of memory loss. Hank's daughter comes from Chicago for a weekend. Contrary to Hank's thoughts, she relates smoothly with Joseph. When she goes back home, Hank gets hints that her manners look like his ex-girlfriend's daughter Carla, thus he suspects that Carla is his kid. Things go on at the trial. Hank manages to prove that it's hard to see the road of the crime scene, but the prosecutor work on Joseph's pride making him convince his feelings that though he can't remember hitting him, he's glad that the victim's dead. Hank pulls out his last trick, bringing out the medication and memory loss. This angered Joseph and he requests to change counsels. But the presiding judge demands Joseph to complete his testimony first with Hank. Asked why did Joseph gave a lenient sentence to Blackwell all those years ago, Joseph reveals the truth that he saw Hank in Blackwell and Blackwell in Mark interchangeably. This grew his mercy to give Blackwell a new chance, and grew his strictness to Hank so that Hank doesn't grow to be like Blackwell. The jury verdicts Joseph as not guilty of murder but guilty of manslaughter, and the presiding judge sentences him to four years in prison. Joseph gets out on parole seven months later and Hank picks him up. They go fishing on a lake and Joseph admit Hank's talent as a lawyer and dies there.The story overall is interesting enough. It combines many diverse drama subjects in one movie; from father-son relationship, childhood nostalgia, cunning lawyer job, and a 'nursing home' story element. Being billed as only a drama genre, the law case is nicely presented as mysterious at the beginning. Also the comedic inserts are also nice.One thing I don't really get though is that those many things have some great deal of focus that the whole movie doesn't really have one main focus. The title says to focus on the law case, but the presence of Samantha's and Lauren's points of the story kind of pulls back the focus from the case and stretches the drama into a big span. This is also not yet mentioning the potential story at Dickham's side.I like how the story depicts Hank's character. Being as hard as his dad Joseph, he again and again abandons him yet again and again returns to his side even swallowing in the rejections from Joseph. Unfortunately this adequate character building doesn't cover the rest of the characters presented. The movie only presents one or two traits for any other character and strangely keeps focusing on those traits. It makes the other characters look like robots or something.The acting parts is a good overall for me. The two Roberts nicely put down their acting on those heated arguments and father-son moments. Downey Jr. still retains his fast talking skill nicely. Except for those father-son moments, he still really looks like a Tony Stark or Sherlock Holmes in character to me, though. Robert Duvall very nicely portrays the old stubborn geezer who once was someone. Dax Shepard nicely adds to the comedic aspect. The other cast members make the story more alive well enough.For me, The Judge (2014) deserves a 6 out of 10 score. It's quite a nice entertainment, a drama with added mystery and comedic aspects. If only story has more stable and narrowed down focus, I think it can be better. One thing for sure though, It's a good break from seeing Robert Downey Jr. as a superhero.</fullReview>

Who would have thought he needed something a little conventional to shine? Maybe it was because this is the kind of genre that has been cheapened for a while as we are treated to a hybrid that combines the slasher film/mystery with a touch of legal seasoning.This film's heart is the law and its effects on the lives of those who believe in it. "The Judge" is the story of two people, their relationship, and how it grows from something pure to something that has decayed over the years, poisoning whatever love and beauty it had before. It has some contrived moments, but they never overpower the basic story like like the gimmicks of the recent "This is Where I Leave" which succumbs to overacting and vulgarity. It has more in common with Grisham's thrillers, without the intrigue. "The Judge" has a more personal focus, and it works out beautifully.Downey plays a successful and ruthless attorney who specializes in defending the guilty. He will work for those who can afford his services and couldn't care less whether they are innocent or not. It is as if he thrives on showing how good he is at thrashing the opposition, bragging how good he is at what he does, and he's never happy because the one compliment he wants is not to be gained so easily.One day he's forced to return to his hometown and face the demons in his past, particularly that father he seems to hate. He only refers to him as "The Judge", and Duvall is icily effective in the way he approaches his sons. He seems to have only love for his recently deceased wife, so we know he has a heart, but why is he so distant and hard on his sons?As the story unfolds, we find out why the father/son relationship is so strained. It's got roots on a couple of tragic development which force both father and soon to react out of disappointment which ironically originates in misunderstanding what is best for each other. Downey quickly begins to get closer to his father, and we see how these two had the stronger st bond in the family, and they get closer as it becomes obvious that time is of the essence here.Enters the legal drama, and Downey has to defend his father in a jury trial to prove his innocence. Things are not easy because the truth might never be revealed. "The Judge" is after all now sitting in the accused's chair, and his testimony is affected by circumstances that prove to be key to the eventual resolution of the case.Throughout the film, Downey does nothing short of spectacular while he is acting. His range has never been more effectively displayed. There is anger, love, happiness, sadness, misery, disappointment, resolve, determination, shock, and just about everything we have seen in great character performances. I couldn't help thinking about Vivien Leigh's work in "Gone With the Wind", as she evolves from the spirited teen into a mature woman who fails to realize the truth until it's too late. I was looking at an actor joining the likes of Streep's magnificent turns, Bale's intense embodiment of a character, leaving nothing untouched, with no hesitation to show the raw sentiments that torture him.This might be his performance that puts him on the company of Day Lewis' "Lincoln", Leigh's "Blanche", Peck's "Atticus", Streep's "Sophie", and some of those iconic roles and performances. In the end we see him contemplating his future by looking at one of this father's office objects, and we realize that we have gone full circle, and that he's about to become what he always feared but he really wanted.</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>Taking a stand, with wilful intent</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Make no mistake, this one's a mainstream flick. Subtle it ain't. I can (kinda) understand that, since the material calls for elaborate beats, some grandstanding, and the template of a sweeping epic. And the actors deliver with the quality of writing available to them, as they only can, by playing to the galleries. Surprisingly, those galleries apparently haven't filled much with audiences. Poor timing, maybe? Or, bad word-of-mouth. In spite of all of the above, a buddy of mine and I ended up making the trek to the multiplex, and ended up having a nice time. There are quite a few occasions when the leads end up eliciting plenty of laughs from the aforementioned galleries, and we ended up laughing with the rest. But, in my opinion, it could all have amounted to much, much more. Casting, and then not utilizing the talent bundled up, esp. in the likes of Vera Farmiga and Vincent D'Onofrio (not to mention Billy Bob Thornton), is nothing short of a crime in itself. I understand you need to focus on the main leads, but what are they if they cannot be showing interacting actively with their (in some cases, respective) environments? This, to me, was a huge mis-step. What else could've been done better? Having a sub-plot that informs various degrees of incest, and then conveniently glossing over that, and what's more, using that to comedic effect, is nothing short of criminal, and what could have been a refreshing look at the way mainstream cinema is going with it depiction of relationships, lapses into something stillborn, and without a payoff that lets characters at least reflect on the consequences of their actions.David Dobkin steps away from the kind of movies he's done with this flick ('Wedding crashers', for one), but he just might need to put in more effort if he wants to be taken seriously. He's got all the template moves that mainstream flick-funding studios need, and might just be toeing their line too, but still needs a trek. My buddy remarked that Downey Jr. needs to reinvent himself and stop playing the stock character that he's been known for, by, perhaps, playing someone more sensitive, and just perhaps more vulnerable so the audience can be in his corner from scene one (that helps). In this one, the writing goes way off the page to make him a (kinda) saint, that doesn't fit in with his character beats. The degree of predictability is too high for a courtroom drama, and someone else remarked during the break that for a movie that's purportedly a courtroom drama, it doesn't spend much time in there. I disagreed, since I felt it was rounding everything off with some balanced time across various scenarios, but did feel that few things were overdone and few other things seemed, for lack of a better word, undercooked.Thomas Newman's soundtrack is adequate, calling attention to itself (not in a bad way) sometimes, but it's too mainstream, and most audiences might find solace there. The technical beats are all there, and that's not a surprise either. All in all, worth the trek to the multiplex, but don't expect to be blown away, though the talent at hand does try to make this more than the mundane fare that's usually dished out.</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>"That's my family Kay, it's not me."</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>This Warner Bros picture is a court and family drama about a slick big-city lawyer who has to return to his small-town home-town. The lawyer is played by Robert Downey Jr. In 'Only You' (1994) he played the charming love interest rejected by Marisa Tomei. Here too, he shows some charm whilst also being a hard-nosed lawyer.Returning to his home-town, the lawyer meets his family again; his two brothers and his father. There is much tension in the family, and this is heightened by an impending court-case, as is shown in the trailer. The emotions are high as family members confront their shared past. There is also some subtle humour in this serious, emotional and moving family drama.Robert Duvall plays the father of the family and he is also the eponymous judge of the film title. Veteran actor Mr Duvall made his film debut in the 1962 film 'To Kill a Mockingbird'. This court-based film is referenced slightly in 'The Judge'. Over the years the versatile Mr Duvall has played a variety of roles in a variety of films. He was 'Lucky' Ned Pepper, the outlaw-cowboy in 'True Grit' 1969. He was also the gangster-lawyer Tom Hagen in 'The Godfather' (1972) and the year after that took the lead role as the gangster Earl Macklin in 'The Outfit' (1973). Mr Duvall again played a lawyer in 'A Civil Action' (1998) and in the same year played the veteran father-figure astronaut in 'Deep Impact'. Like in those 1998 films, here again in 'The Judge', Mr Duvall plays a character of much depth and complexity. The performance is both perfect and poignant.The judge's two other sons are played well by Vincent D'Onofrio and Jeremy Strong. They, and Mr Downey and Mr Duvall all interact well, and in a believable way, as a family. As does Emma Tremblay playing a member of the third generation of the family. From youngest to oldest, realistic acting, gives us a realistic family in a realistic situation. From youngest to oldest, family members give advice, and learn lessons, as do we. We choose our friends but not our family.Mr D'Onofrio playing the eldest son of the judge, like Mr Duvall, also gives a rather poignant performance. In the popular long-running TV Series 'Law &amp; Order: Criminal Intent (2001-2011), we viewers gradually learned about the complex character of the much-loved Detective Robert Goren. A portrayal, that is perhaps only equalled by James Gandolfini's 'Tony Soprano'. Here, in this smaller part, Mr D'Onofrio plays a character who is necessarily a bit of an enigma. Great passion is hinted at, but what his true feelings are, is a matter of interpretation.Being also a court drama, there are other legal figures too. Billy Bob Thornton gives a solid and believable performance which also exhibits some depth. By contrast, Dax Shepard is merely a comic McGuffin whose role is somewhat like that of Austin Pendleton in 'My Cousin Vinny' (1992), which starred Joe Pesci and Marisa Tomei. He gives us very much needed comic breaks in a very serious film, though not one totally devoid of humour. There are at least one or two genuinely laugh-out-loud moments in some of the scenes and situations. Comedy and pathos are also supplied in some non-court scenes with Vera Farmiga and Leigton Meester.Much of the film is from the POV of Mr Downey's character. We learn things when he does, sometimes. Other times, we learn more about his early life in his home-town, as it is later revealed to us in the film. Thus we see all the other characters in this film as they interact with him. Mr Downey gives a great performance, throughout this film, as we watch his character develop. It should also be said that this is the first feature release for the Team Downey Production Company, a label that says what it does on the tin. Robert Downey Jr was an executive producer, and his wife Susan Downey was also a producer, thus Team Downey.There is great acting throughout 'The Judge'. The film, rated 15 in the UK, is an adult-themed court and family drama, that is suitable for all persons of appropriate age, who are members of a family.Moving and emotional. 10/10.</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>Flawed but impactful</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Quickie Review:After the death of his mother, lawyer Hank Palmer (Robert Downey Jr.) returns to his hometown. During the visit his estranged father, the judge Joseph Palmer (Robert Duvall) is suspected of murder. As the case unfolds and the wounds in the family revealed, Hank has the opportunity to mend those broken relationships and reconnect with his family. The Judge is emotional and deeply touching, and it's all thanks to the acting powerhouse Robert Duvall and Robert Downey Jr. That alone is reason enough to watch the movie. It is slightly too long and some characters are not well utilized, but as a whole this is a solid movie.Full Review:Just looking at the cast I knew I had to give The Judge a shot, and for the most part I was right. Like I previously mentioned the two leads are what makes the movie work. On the other hand there are some cast members I would've liked to have seen more of and others a little less.Robert Downey Jr. and Robert Duvall are the ones who brought the somewhat clichéd estranged father story-plot to another level. Both their characters are extremely stubborn, clinging on to old events as an excuse to how they behave. Hence whenever they are at odds with each other they bring an emotional weight to the scene. So much so that you actually feel the weight, and you might want some room to breathe because of how tense a scene was. Fortunately the audience does get some break from time to time thanks to its pacing and subtle humor sprinkled where appropriate. Also you are not always bogged down by the family drama, there is also a thriller element to the movie that is engaging during the court room sessions. Downey's charisma really shines during the court hearings because of the command he holds in the room.Where the movie fails is how it handles some of the supporting cast. Vera Farmiga's character for example is supposed to be the love interest for Downey. A lot of time is actually spent on their relationship. But that subplot keeps going around in circles and never leads to anything. Perhaps it is meant to show other relationships Hank is mending, but you won't care about that as much as how things are between him and his actual family. So to be honest that whole subplot and character could've been removed and it wouldn't have made a difference. Another example of mishandling a character is the one played by Billy Bob Thornton. He plays the lawyer Downey is up against, but the entire movie Thornton felt more like spectator than a serious threat to the leads. I would've loved to have seen them both clash heads in the court room, but unfortunately that never happens.Despite the mishandling of the supporting cast, The Judge is a strong movie. There have been many estranged family stories in the past, but rarely has the emotional depth that this movie has. So if you are on the edge or undecided, I'd recommend giving it a shot.Check out more on my movie review blog The Stub Collector: http://thestubcollector.wordpress.com/</fullReview>

		<username>viewsonfilm.com</username>
		<score>0.7</score>
		<shortDescription>VIEWS ON FILM review of The Judge</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Taking place in Southern Indiana (you can tell because the landscape is full of foothills) and directed by a guy who is known chiefly for making comedies, The Judge is a long yet briskly paced courtroom drama. The overall plot concerns a hot shot Chicago lawyer who must defend his own father (a longtime judge presiding in a small town via the Hoosier state) against a stone cold murder charge. This mean-spirited, resentful (you could throw in spiteful too) lawyer flies into town initially to go to his mother's funeral. He ends up staying longer than needed when his father's dim-witted counsel can't handle the intensity of the case, a case involving a death by hit-and-run. Robert Downey Jr. plays the big city attorney (Henry "Hank" Palmer) while the legendary Robert Duvall plays his tough as nails, not to mention bullheaded judge father (Joseph Palmer).As an originally conceived film with a title that averts to something John Grisham would approve of, The Judge didn't really remind me of any other dramas except for its mild sentiment towards certain clichés. You know, the aspects of a rich, well-off lad who comes back to his small hometown after he abandons his family and leaves behind the high school sweetheart that he once loved. And oh yeah, you can also throw in the cranky old timer who wasn't a good father and may or may not be a recovering alcoholic (a la 1982's The Verdict). No matter though. In essence, this is an absorbing, chaotic, messy, yet powerfully realized downer of a movie. It's depressing in its dysfunctional family dynamic and its ending is clearly anti-Hollywood (really not what you'd except). However, there is never a dull moment and at roughly two hours and twenty plus minutes, there's a lot of movie in this movie. You get what you paid for and then some.The acting is top drawer, all down the line. Downey Jr. and Duvall have only been in the same flick one other time (1998's The Gingerbread Man) and clearly don't look related. But they work well together. Their performances in the lead roles hit you like a ton of bricks. Watching them spar in scene after gut wrenching scene, you feel as if they were actually bear and cub in real life. It's truly intense. And I gotta admit, Robert Downey Jr.'s "Hank" was tough to put up with for the majority of the running time. There's a lot of pent up anger there. There's also lots of moodiness, regret, and narcissism going on. Downey Jr. with his fast talking mannerisms and blatant Tony Starkness (you know, his character from Iron Man), looks like he's going to a bad place with this character. You feel like he could just explode at any minute. His performance is no doubt brilliant but gee-whiz, he could have possibly lightened up a bit. As for the rest of cast, they are solid especially Billy Bob Thornton exuding a sort of menacing cool. He plays the prosecutor in Duvall's character's case who is bent on justice (and the prospect of issuing a murder one charge to boot). Finally, we have Vera Farmiga effectively channeling the token, long lost love interest and Vincent D'Onofrio riffing off his The Break Up role by playing "Hank's" otherwise nervous older brother (Glen Palmer). Oh and by the way, did I mention the hard R language these characters carry off with totally relentless aplomb? This ain't no People's Court. This is indeed 12 Angry Men (and you can maybe count some woman too).In conclusion, The Judge is pretty meaty entertainment despite a few hiccups along the way. I mean I could have done without a couple of scenes involving projectile vomiting and the sight of runny poop coming out of an 80 plus year old man (note to director David Dobkin: you're not making The Change-Up here. You're making a full-fledged, human drama). Also, I couldn't figure out why Downey Jr.'s "Hank" stayed at his father's storage room (at the Harper family home) during the opening funeral. I mean initially they hated each other, right? So I'm thinking that "Hank" could have at least found a hotel room (even in the small, fictitious hick town of Carlinville, Indiana). Regardless, this early fall release gets a recommendation from me. If you decide to take in a viewing, remind yourself that this film is not just a farce about the fate of a dying man through a handful of jurors. Think of what's on screen as a rekindling of fathers and sons by which the characters have extremely strong personalities and for the most part, are just too bad for each other. My final verdict on The Judge: Guilty, as in you're guilty if you don't check it out at a theater near you.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<username>David Ferguson (fergusontx@gmail.com)</username>
		<score>0.6</score>
		<shortDescription>Hooker in a Hot Tub</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Greetings again from the darkness. It's hard to beat a good on screen courtroom drama for tension and conflict. Despite centering around a long time judge accused of manslaughter and being defended by his estranged son, a hotshot defense attorney, this one eschews courtroom action in favor of uncomfortable and explosive family dynamics. And thanks to the acting abilities of Robert Duvall and Robert Downey Jr, that's a good thing.Mr. Downey's Iron Man/Tony Stark character has ingrained in movie goers his motor-mouthed smart-aleck persona that fits very well with the lacking-a-conscience defense attorney who only defends the type of white collar criminals who can afford his unmatched courtroom savvy. When Hank (Downey) returns home for the funeral of his mother, we quickly witness the lack of relationship with his demanding-perfection father (Duvall), and the historical details slow-drip for the next couple of hours.Hank's older brother (Vincent D'Onofrio) was once a promising baseball player whose career was cut short after an automobile accident. Hank's younger brother (Jeremy Strong, who played Lee Harvey Oswald in Parkland) is a mentally handicapped young man attached to his video camera. Hank also (of course) runs into his high school sweetheart (Vera Farmiga) and her daughter (Leighton Meester), as a reminder of what he left behind in his quaint hometown when he chose fortune and big city life.John Grisham has made a career, actually two (books and movies) about lawyers and courtrooms. As you might imagine, director David Dobkin (Wedding Crashers, Fred Claus) doesn't have Grisham's eye and ear for the courtroom, so the script slips into manipulative melodrama during the trial (think Grisham-lite). But the scenes between Duvall and Downey more than make up for the fluffy parts. The kitchen confrontation and the bathroom sequence couldn't be any more different, or any more powerful. One is the exorcism of a parent-child relationship gone bad, and the other is a vivid depiction of old age and disease.This is old-fashioned mainstream movie-making. It's about relationships and family and personality and life choices. There are no explosions or CGI or car chases. Even the key crime isn't shown. It's also not breaking any new ground, and if not for the acting, could be just another TV movie. A perfect example is Vera Farmiga, who brings an edge to a role that otherwise would be superfluous. Same with Hank's brothers. Both roles are severely underwritten, but D'Onofrio and Strong somehow make them work. Billy Bob Thornton brings an element to an otherwise not-believable role as a slick special prosecutor wearing $1000 suits. Even Dax Sheperd plays his comic relief country attorney in an understated (for him) manner.Other support work is provided by Ken Howard as the judge, Emma Trembley (Hank's daughter), Balthazar Getty as the deputy with a grudge, Grace Zabriske as the victim's vengeance seeking mother, David Krumholtz as a District Attorney, and Denis O'Hare as Duvall's doctor.In better hands, the script could have become much sharper and the film much crisper. Prepare for cheese and schmaltz, but it's difficult to imagine more fun than watching Duvall and Downey square off. If you stay for closing credits, you'll hear Willie Nelson wobbly warble through a Coldplay song.</fullReview>
	</review>

The work is so flawless that never ever you will know that are actually acting! Hank is Judge's 2nd son and lives separately, visits his dad and his 2 brothers when his mom dies. On the same night Judge is accused of a murder, that too of a convict whom he has sentenced jail 20 years back. Hank is a very successful lawyer and after his dad tries out a new boy to be his lawyer for the case, Hank takes up the case. The movie is funny (hilarious at times), romantic, dramatic and has a lot of elements of suspense. Along with some fantastic performance by supporting actors, both the Roberts will keep you glued to the plot. Most of the 2nd half is full of courtroom drama and as it unfolds every scene gets very exciting. Disclosure: I love courtroom dramas. Hank's relationship with his dad has gone through enough ups and down, the aggressive discussion between the dad and the son in the middle of the movie is very moving and touching, again fine actors at their best! Movie duration being 2 hours and 20 mins, is slightly long but will never bore you. I highly recommend you watch this one.</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>justice and family</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>The Judge is a rather classical combination of family drama and court drama, enjoying a couple of fine actors in lead roles that will land them some place in or close to the area of the Academy Awards candidacies. It's solid but rather conventional cinematography, and as I understand the first output of a new production enterprise where Robert Downey Jr. is placing some of the money earned by the best-paid actor in Hollywood today. As a capitalist Mr. Downey Jr. seems to like conservative, low risk investment.Hank Palmer (Downey) is a successful and filthy lawyer who helps people rich enough to pay him to get away in the American courts of law even if they are obviously guilty. When his mother dies he returns for what should be a one or two days unwanted family reunion in the mid-west city of birth too small for his ambitions. As much as he dislikes the trip he is also rather disliked by a quite non-functional family run by a tyrannical father (Robert Duvall) who is also the judge practically running the place, a man admired by half of the town (the good one?) and hated by the other half (the bad one?). When his father is accused of murder Hank will find himself defending him for once with truth and justice as goals, while all the past he was running of for the last two decades risks to overwhelm him.The relation between father and son is enhanced by the intense acting of Downey Jr. and Duvall which may both compete for the Academy Awards a few months from now. Director David Dobkin seems to have previously dealt with comedies only, The Judge being his first exploration of a 'serious' thematic. He make a few mistakes on the road - the film is too long, and the treatment of the subject too heavy and conventional to my taste. It's not bad cinema, it is just so expected. I hope that better contenders will show up on the road to the Awards between now and February 22, 2015, the date of the ceremony at the Dolby Theater in Hollywood, LA.</fullReview>
